Received 
      6th December 2021
    , Accepted 21st February 2022First published on 21st February 2022AbstractThermoelectric (TE) materials have attracted tremendous research interests over the past few decades, due to their application in power generation technology from waste heat, almost without producing any pollution in the environment. The energy conversion efficiency depends on a dimensionless quantity called the “Figure of merit” (ZT) of the material. The higher value of ZT indicates a material to be a good thermoelectric. So the aim is to find materials with a high ZT value and can commercially be used in devices like thermoelectric generators (TEGs) or thermoelectric coolers (TECs). Some decades ago, the benchmark value of ZT for the available TE materials was ∼1. Still, the application of various novel strategies made it possible to set the target to achieve ZT > 2 or even higher. This review will discuss the traditional and modern strategies for the generic improvement of thermoelectric materials and the progress in thermoelectric performance of Tin and Germanium chalcogenides (SnTe, SnSe, SnS, GeTe, GeSe, and GeS-based materials) over the past few years. Lead chalcogenides have already been one of the best representative materials for TE applications in mid and high-temperature regions for the past few decades. But the toxicity of Pb and related ecological problems restrict their usage for commercial purposes. Sn- and Ge-chalcogenides belong to the same class of (IV–VI) Pb-chalcogenides but cause no serious environmental issues. This article will provide an overview of the progress of Sn- and Ge-chalcogenides as TE materials.Medha RakshitMedha Rakshit received her B. Her current research is focused on synthesis and characterization of nanomaterials (inorganic and organic) including Graphene and Carbon nanotubes and composites for green energy generation and environmental remediation.
1. Introduction
Nowadays, demand for energy in human life is increasing by leaps and bounds, but our conventional energy sources are minimal, so nonconventional energy sources are drawing more attention. Environment pollution is also a big problem now, so pollution-free energy generation or green energy generation is preferable. Much of the total consumed energy in various modern industries and process plants is dissipated as waste heat. A proportion of this waste heat can be converted into electricity by applying thermoelectric power generation technology. Thermoelectric conversion efficiency is strongly related to a dimensionless quantity, called the “figure of merit” (ZT) of material, and is defined by the expression, (1)where S-is the Seebeck coefficient or thermopower of the material, σ-is the electrical conductivity of the material, κ-is the thermal conductivity of the material, T-is the absolute temperature and the whole quantity in the numerator [S2σ]-is called the “power factor” of the material. ZT is a consequence of the contest between electronic and thermal transport in a material. TE devices are solid state heat engines. The devices have no moving parts, are highly reliable, and the operation is quiet. The building block of a TE device is called TE-element. Each element consists of an n-type and a p-type leg, made of TE materials. Over a hundred such pairs of TE elements are connected electrically in series and thermally in parallel conditions in a TE device, as shown in Fig. 1 and 2. The power generation efficiency1–6 (ηTE) of a TEG is defined by eqn (2) (2)where Tc and Th are the temperatures of cold and hot sides respectively of a TE generator, Tavg-is the average temperature between Tc and Th, ηc-represents the Carnot efficiency and is expressed as  and ZTavg-is the average over the temperature-dependent ZT values between Th and Tc of both n-type and p-type legs. Higher the value of ZT better the material is for TE application.1–6 It can clearly be understood from the given expression of ZT-that to increase the value of ZT-the values of S and σ-should be increased and κ-should be decreased.
 Fig. 1  (a) TE leg in a TE-element, (b) TE-element inside a TEG. 
 Fig. 2  (a) Array of TE-elements in a TE device, (b), and (c) TE device. 
Materials with low electron scattering, therefore high electrical conductivity, and strong phonon scattering are preferable for better TE application. Based on these requirements, crystalline materials of the “phonon-glass electron-crystal” (PGEC) type was proposed as suitable TE materials by Slack.7 According to this proposal, the TE materials should possess an intriguing structure that effectively allows the propagation of charge carriers while blocking phonon propagation for better TE performance. Depending on this proposal, various new strategies, concepts, and material systems have been established in past decades. Mainly Cu-based chalcogenides have emerged as a new class of promising TE materials based on this concept.8–15 It is not an easy task to optimize S, σ, and κ according to the requirement because these three quantities are strongly correlated with each other. No quantity can be modified without affecting at least any other quantity in a negative way. So first, we have to decouple these quantities and then modify them. Rapid improvement in the ZT (>1) value of TE materials has resulted from various newly invented strategies. The creation of nanostructured materials made it possible to improve TE performance tremendously.16–22 Materials in low dimensional forms, such as superlattices (2D structures), nanowires (1D structures), quantum dots (0D structures), etc., can achieve a considerably higher ZT, that was beyond expectations from their traditional bulk forms. The new variable of length scale comes into play with a reduction in the dimensionality of the material, which effectively controls materials' property.18 Thermoelectric properties of traditional bulk materials have been improved by introducing the distribution of nanoscale constituents in the entire sample. This way, to achieve better TE applications, low-dimensional and bulk materials were brought together.17,18 Not all nanostructured materials are efficiently applicable for large-scale commercial uses as they are expensive and slow to be fabricated or can usually be fabricated in a small quantity. Among various nanostructured materials, bulk nanostructured materials can be fabricated via bulk processes rather than those costly and slow nanofabrication processes and are most promising for large-scale commercial purposes.21
Not only inorganic but also organic TE materials are very popular nowadays. The ZT for organic TE materials [polyaniline (PANI), polyacetylene (PA), polypyrrole (PPy), polylactic acid (PLA), polythiophene (PTH), poly(3,4-ethylenedioxythiophene) (PEDOT), poly(3,4-ethylenedioxythiohene):poly(styrenesulfonate) (PEDOT:PSS), etc.] are usually lower than that for inorganic TE materials due to their low electrical conductivity, Seebeck coefficient, and PF. Doped, dedoped organic materials and organic/inorganic nanocomposite materials23–27 show remarkable TE performance. Bi2Te3,25,26 Bi2Se3,24etc., are efficient inorganic compounds used in organic–inorganic composite materials for TE applications. Organic TE materials have the advantage of low density, lightweight, flexibility, low κlattice and can easily be fabricated on a large scale. On the other hand, inorganic TE materials have a high Seebeck coefficient and electric conductivity. To take advantage of both classes of materials, “hybrid organic–inorganic thermoelectrics” is introduced in the field of TE materials.
Among inorganic metal chalcogenides, the IV–VI metal (Pb, Sn, Ge) chalcogenide materials have some inherent properties that make them suitable for TE applications. Firstly, metal chalcogenide materials have higher atomic weight, which is essential to reduce thermal conductivity. Secondly, the less covalent nature of the bonds arising from low electronegativity increases the PF. Thirdly, these materials can be doped easily and made n-type or p-type according to the need, which is essential to use the materials in TEGs or TECs. Fourthly, these materials can be formed in different structural forms and dimensions. These inherent properties open ample scope for improving their TE performance using various modern and traditional strategies. For the past few decades, Pb-chalcogenides have been among the best representatives of inorganic TE materials for TE applications in mid and high-temperature regions. Still, environmental concerns due to the toxicity of Pb are shrinking their large-scale usage. So, the usage of Pb-free metal chalcogenides in various fields, including electrodes, LEDs, solar cells, memory devices, TE devices, etc., is increasing day by day. Sn- and Ge-chalcogenides (SnTe, SnSe, SnS, GeTe, GeSe, GeS) belong to the same class of (IV–VI) Pb-chalcogenides and have already been proved to be promising TE materials. Tellurium (Te) is the heaviest non-radioactive representative among the chalcogens, and selenium (Se) stands next. Among non-radioactive chalcogens, Te has the least electronegativity (2.1, Pauling scale), and then comes Se (2.4, Pauling scale). Lesser ionic bonds for tellurides and selenides lead to enhanced carrier mobility that helps to achieve high electrical conductivity. Reduction in thermal conductivity and increment of electrical conductivity is advantageous to improve thermoelectric performance. However, the toxicity of Te/Se would be a matter to investigate for their practical application in devices. Many excellent recent reviews reported until now focused on the TE and other properties of any particular metal chalcogenide material or the overall progress of TE materials.28–31 In this review, we aim to provide an overall view on the various novel and traditional strategies to improve TE performance of materials and the outcomes of applying those strategies on the tin (Sn) and germanium (Ge) chalcogenides to improve their TE performance. From the comprehensive study, we predicted some new possible routes to improve the TE properties of those materials further and more effectively use them in green energy generation.
The general strategies to improve the TE performance will be discussed in two parts. The first part will contain strategies to increase the power factor, and the later one will include strategies to decrease the thermal conductivity.
2. Enhancement of power factor (PF)
The power factor (PF) of a thermoelectric material consists of the electrical conductivity (σ) and the Seebeck coefficient (S) of that material. Electrical conductivity (σ) is a measure of the ability of a material to conduct electricity. Solving the Boltzmann equation, one finds the resulting formula for the electrical conductivity of a single band as, (3)where, g(E)-is the density of states (DOS), τ-is the relaxation time, f0-Boltzmann distribution function in equilibrium, m*- effective mass of charge carrier.
The Seebeck coefficient is also called “thermopower” or “thermoelectric power”. Seebeck coefficient provides information about the magnitude of the induced thermoelectric voltage due to the Seebeck effect by applying temperature gradient across that material. It is convenient to define the Seebeck coefficient in terms of Fermi energy to analyze the quantity in band structure level. Diffusion of carriers is not the only cause of the overall Seebeck effect in a material.20 Though the most significant part of the Seebeck effect comes from the diffusion of carriers. Using the Boltzmann transport equation, we can write the Seebeck coefficient for a single band as, (4)

Eqn (4) states that for a single band material, S-is a measure of the energy difference between the average energy (〈E〉) of mobile carriers and the Fermi energy (EF) when τ is a constant parameter.
The Seebeck coefficient for a single band with a typical parabolic density of states19,20,32,33 and assuming carriers to obey classical M–B statistics can be expressed as eqn (5) (5)
The (+) sign is for the VB, while the (−) sign is for the CB, D = dimensionality of the material, q = charge of the carrier, kB = Boltzmann constant, η = reduced Fermi energy = r = scattering parameter (+3/2-for ionized impurity scattering, −1/2-for acoustic phonon scattering, 0-for neutral impurity scattering).34–36 If we take 3D-bulk material then, D = 3 and eqn (5) takes the form, (6)
Similarly, for electrical conductivity, the expression is defined as eqn (7) (7)where, τ0-is a constant, and τ = τ0Er
This assumption is applicable till the carrier concentration is low (non-degenerate regime). When carrier concentration is high enough (degenerate regime), F–D statistics should define various transport parameters rather than classical M–B statistics. From extensive reported data, the ideal carrier concentration for suitable TE materials is observed to lie in a range from 1019 cm−3 to 1021 cm−3, as depicted in Fig. 3, which is sufficient to shift the discussion from classical to F–D statistics.
 Fig. 3  In the non-degenerate semiconductor regime, κel is negligible, and Z-increases with n due to an increase in S. In the degenerate semiconductor regime, Z-reaches a peak when n = noptimum, and [S2σ] is maximized. In the metallic regime, κlattice is negligible, and Z-declines with increasing n due to reduction in S, and increases in κel. Reprinted from ref. 20. Copyright (2010), with permission from Elsevier. 
In this case, S-is expressed as eqn (8). (8)where,  = Fermi integral of the order −x. For 3D-bulk material, eqn (8) becomes (9)
In the degenerate regime, σ-is expressed as eqn (10) (10)
In the metallic regime S-is given by the Mott expression37–40 which is derived from derivative of the energy-dependent electrical conductivity σ(E) = n(E)qμ(E) with respect to the energy at Fermi energy [E = EF] as eqn (11) (11)where, n(E) = f(E)g(E) (12)n(E) = carrier concentration at energy level E, f(E) = Fermi function at energy E, μ(E) = mobility of charge carrier at energy E,  = DOS effective mass, and h = Planck's constant. (13)

Eqn (13) shows that there exist two mechanisms that can increase S, i.e., (i) to increase the energy-dependence of n(E) and (ii) to increase the energy dependence of μ(E). For 3D-bulk materials, eqn (13) can be simplified as, (14)

Eqn (14) states that materials having high -have a high S, thus beneficial to improve ZT. But in the actual case, large DOS due to flat bands leads to a large transport effective mass, which also causes the lowering of mobility (μ), so the PF [S2σ] cannot exceed a particular limiting value. From this discussion, it can be concluded that better TE performance requires lower band effective mass.41 Flat bands and their effects will be discussed later in Section 2.4. From eqn (14), we see that S-decreases if n-is increased. On the other hand, an increase in n is related to an increase in EF-and average energy both. However, the average energy increases relatively slowly than EF. So, in eqn (4), when τ is a constant parameter, then S-continuously decreases as the n-increases. An increase in temperature can also increase carrier concentration hence a decrease in S-and vice versa. Variation of Seebeck coefficient with n is shown in Fig. 4. In an extrinsic semiconductor, the increase in temperature may not significantly increase the carrier concentration, which is fixed to the dopant concentration and does not increase the average energy. In this case, the Fermi level is reduced, and S-is increased. At sufficiently high temperature, the semiconductor may become intrinsic and exhibits a decrease in S-with increasing temperature. This is because both conduction and VBs are now relevant, and the Seebeck coefficient of opposite sign in each band leads to a smaller net value.20
 Fig. 4  Schematic variation of Seebeck coefficient (S), electrical conductivity (σ) and PF(S2σ) with carrier concentration. Reprinted from ref. 29. Copyright (2016), with permission from Elsevier. 
2.1 Increase in local DOS and distortions in DOS by the resonant level formation

S-can be increased if a local increase of DOS in a narrow energy range can be achieved by some techniques.42 Mahan and Sofo expressed ZT-as a functional of transport distribution and found that the form of the distribution function should be a Dirac delta function (2.4 kBT above or below EF) to maximize ZT, but this exact requirement is not usually fulfilled because the local variance in the sample's composition may cause the broadening of the delta function, and the DOS has other terms in addition to a single delta function. To correct this problem, they combined two different single delta functions as DOS functions. Still, the resulting ZT was even lower than the previously calculated ZT from the single delta function. Then, instead of two delta functions, a constant background term was added with the function of DOS as given in eqn (15). The correction term was required due to the presence of other electronic states that do not create the delta-function-like DOS in solid.43 (15)And the transport distribution function was defined as, (16)where, C- is a constant,  About 10% contribution of the background, corresponding to a = 0.1, reduces ZT to 25% of its original value for a = 0.43 So the aim is to find out such materials where the distribution of energy carriers is as narrow as possible with high carrier velocity along the direction of the applied electric field. This idealized mathematical model has been realized in materials having either intrinsic narrowband or resonant state around impurity atom. Resonant levels are formed when the CB or VB of the host semiconductor resonates with one energy level of a localized atom in a semiconductor matrix.42 But resonant-state formation by impurities also has a negative impact on σ, because impurities remarkably reduce the carrier mobility due to additional carrier scattering.
However, distortion in DOS is an effective way to enhance the Seebeck coefficient. Different mechanisms to distort DOS are schematically represented in Fig. 5. The formation of resonant impurity levels distorts the DOS effectively. The presence of impurities can heavily modify the TE properties of a material.44–48 If there are quasi-local states called “resonant levels” of impurity atoms in any allowed energy band of the host material, some extraordinary phenomena can occur. Firstly, carriers can jump on those quasi-local states from the band. After residing there for some time, the carriers ejected back to the band with a different momentum; this is called ‘Resonant scattering’. The finite lifetime of the carriers in the impurity state broadens the energy level (though the broadening may be caused by different reasons also).7 So, the carriers with energies close to the level will be predominantly scattered, making the resonant scattering relaxation time strongly energy-dependent if the broadening is not too significant. This increased energy dependence can increase or decrease τ-and hence S-accordingly.
 Fig. 5  Mechanisms used to distort the DOS in solids and to increase their thermopower, (a) two-band conduction, (b) resonant impurity level formation, (c) band hybridization, (d) dilute Kondo. Reproduced from ref. 42 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry. 
Resonant scattering causes a strong electron energy filtering effect that enhances the Seebeck coefficient at low temperatures where the electron–phonon interactions are weaker. Secondly, these impurity electrons are in extended states (plane wave-like wave function); they take part in electricity and heat conduction and contribute to the thermopower.42,47 If the resonance level lies below the chemical potential (μ) in a degenerate sample(E < μ) Seebeck coefficient is increased and vice versa. The presence of resonance level affects both terms in eqn (13). When impurities are added to metals, the distortion in DOS is lower hence, -is very small, and the second term  is prominent. In the case of semiconductors -is more important.
Heremans et al.49 demonstrated experimentally that ZT of Tl-doped PbTe could be doubled by increasing S-via introducing additional Tl-impurity levels. The density of electronic states pure PbTe and Tl-doped PbTe is shown in Fig. 6a. Enhancement of S-through a distortion of the electronic DOS resulted in a doubling of ZT in p-type PbTe to ∼1.5 at 773 K, as shown in Fig. 6b. The first experimental evidence of resonant level formation in Bi2Te3via Sn doping was reported by Jaworski et al.50 Here, Sn-sufficiently distorts the DOS of the upper VB to result in a substantial increase in S. Hsu et al.51 worked on a class of novel quaternary compounds AgPbmSbTe2+m and achieved a large figure of merit ZT ∼ 2.2 at 800 K. In this case, resonant states appeared near the VBM and CBM of bulk PbTe when Ag and Sb replaced Pb.52 In a report by Simonson et al.53 introduction of resonant states by the inclusion of V (less than 1%)-dopant in half-Heusler alloy Hf0.75Zr0.25NiSn resulted in an enhanced TE performance. Deng et al.54 observed a peak in DOS near band edge in experimentally synthesized 2D PTTA [poly(tetrathienoanthracene)]. They assumed the presence of quasi-one-dimensional states as the cause of the unique nature of DOS. Electron–phonon interactions can also induce distortions of the DOS; some of these phenomena are “phonon-drag”,55–62 “Nielsen–Taylor pseudo-phonon-drag effect”,63 “polaron mass enhancements”,64etc.
 Fig. 6  (a) Schematic representation of the density of electron states of the VB of pure PbTe (dashed line) contrasted to that of Tl–PbTe in which a Tl-related level increases the density of states. ZT is optimized when the Fermi energy EF of the holes in the band falls in the energy range ER of the distortion. (b) The ZT values for Tl0.02Pb0.98Te (red circles) and Tl0.01Pb0.99Te (black squares). Reproduced from ref. 49. 
2.2 Relaxation time modulation for charge carriers
When τ is not a constant but a function of E in eqn (4), then the average energy should be weighted by τ to determine the value of S. Charge carriers in a semiconductor may be scattered by stationary defects like impurities and dislocations or by dynamic defects, e.g., electrons, holes, and acoustic phonons. When scattering of charge carriers is not confined within a single energy band, intraband or intravalley scatterings are elastic scattering accompanied by the emission or absorption of the longitudinal mode of acoustic phonons. On the other hand, interband or intervalley scatterings are inelastic and accompanied by the absorption or emission of optical phonons, which occur at high temperatures or high electric fields.34 Bardeen and Shockley developed the deformation potential (DP) method of calculating τ for the nonpolar acoustic phonons scattering, leading to a final expression for τ,65 as given in eqn (17) (17)where, Ξ-is the DP coefficient, the critical parameter determining the strength of carrier scattering by acoustic phonons. The parameter is defined as the change of band energy (ΔE) per relative volume change (ΔV) of the crystal. vl-is the longitudinal velocity of sound waves, and ρ-is density. If we talk about the cases where scattering by static imperfections or ionized impurity scattering is dominant, then according to Conwell and Weisskopf, using bare Coulomb potential as the perturbing Hamiltonian τ-can be expressed as44 (18)where, ε-is the permittivity of the material,  and 2rm = NI−1/3. Brooks and Herring66 later modified this expression by using screened Coulomb potential for those cases where the carrier concentration is too high to screen the impurities. So from the above expressions, we can conclude that the τ can be expressed in terms of the temperature and carrier energy [see Table 1], which is proportional to (T−pEr). An increase in (β = −p + r)-will increase the energy dependence of τ and hence will enhance S-value. So the value of S is higher when ionized impurity scattering (β = +3/2) dominates over neutral impurity (β = 0) and acoustic phonon scattering (β = −3/2-for DP scattering, and β = −1/2-for piezoelectric scattering).35,36,45

So, the Seebeck coefficient can be modified by manipulating τ-and changing the dominance of different scattering processes. A method of manipulation of τ-is through resonant scattering by impurity and defect centers.7,46,48,67,68 According to Ravich et al.,7 when τ and the mean free path (l) are strongly energy-dependent,69 then carrier scattering may show some selective nature, i.e., scattering of the carriers with particular energy are more likely than those with a different energy. ‘Resonant scattering by impurity and defect centers’ and ‘scattering by potential barriers at grain and crystallite boundaries’ exhibit a considerably higher selectivity than ‘Coulomb scattering by charged impurity centers’.7 Presence of potential barrier comparable to the height of EF-can also make τ strongly energy-dependent. Carriers with energy lower than the barrier height are excluded from the carrier flow, and those with higher energy are allowed to pass through the barrier.
2.3 Lowering of the deformation potential (DP)
Chasmar70 first introduced the term “Thermoelectric quality factor” (B). This dimensionless quantity is direct proportionally related to the ZT of a TE material and can also be expressed as70,71 (19)where, Cl = average longitudinal elastic moduli, and Ξ = DP coefficient. Eqn (19) leads to the conclusion that lowering Ξ can be an effective strategy to obtain high ZT materials other than having a large NV and a low  Low Ξ-is also expected for high μ of charge carriers and a weak scattering of carriers by acoustic phonons. Recently, a high-throughput (HT) computational method,72 an efficient tool to screen high-performance functional materials, has been employed to identify suitable TE materials. The calculation involves the DP method under the single band model to evaluate the carrier relaxation time (electronic) and the Grüneisen parameter (or lattice anharmonicity) by computing the elastic properties. In particular, out of 668 binary chalcogenides, the study screens 50 promising thermoelectric materials with excellent electronic properties and intrinsically ultralow thermal conductivities.72 Wang et al. reported ZT ∼ 1.2 at 850 K for n-type PbSe due to the smaller DP coefficient of the conduction than the valence band, resulting in high carrier mobility.73 Low DP coefficient caused better ZT in p-type FeV0.6 Nb0.4 Sb sample also.74
2.4 Lowering of band effective mass and increment of band degeneracy
An increase in DOS near EF of bulk TE materials is possible by various band engineering techniques. Three types of band engineering techniques used to enhance TE performance are based on the compositional tuning of TE materials. They are named resonant state formation,42,46–53,68,75 band flattening,76,77 and band convergence,78–81 respectively. A high ZT via the resonant-state formation has already been discussed. The “optimal figure of merit” (ZToptimal) depends on “Thermoelectric quality factor” (B), where 70,71,74,82 The DOS effective mass can be expressed as,  Where, NV-is the band degeneracy, -is the band effective mass and  with m1, m2 and m3 as mass components along three principal axes in a 3D bulk sample. In an isotropic band  and  Here  is called inertial effective mass and expressed by the given relation, 32 So, we can see that large  can arise due to either a large number of conducting bands (NV) or by flat bands (with high ). The flatness of the bands influences the Seebeck coefficient and mobility oppositely. A high Seebeck coefficient originates from the high total density of states (DOS) effective mass at a given carrier concentration. So, higher  or flatter bands are beneficial to improve the Seebeck coefficient. If, the charge transport by acoustic phonon is dominant, then  So using isotropic band approximation, we get 70,71,74,82–94 Thus, it can be concluded that heavy band effective mass harms TE performance as it reduces mobility and hence ZT. Pei et al.75 synthesized La- and I-doped PbTe by the melting, quenching, annealing, and hot pressing methods. They demonstrated that a lower effective mass leads to a high PF and thus excellent TE performance in n-PbTe. Lowering of effective mass was achieved either by doping or by adjusting the temperature. I-doped PbTe has a 20% lower effective mass than La-doped PbTe. The higher effective mass in La-doped PbTe is presumably due to the CB flattening, and it caused a 20% reduction of PF in La-dopes PbTe. On the other hand, a temperature-induced 40% increase in effective mass resulted in a 35% decrease in peak PF for both La- and I- doped PbTe.
On the other hand, valleys with considerably high value NV or multiple degenerate valleys are beneficial to large  without deterioration of μ.33,74,78 Band degeneracy NV is increased by some causes. One is when multiple bands have band extrema around the same energy or almost within the negligible difference in energy (orbital degeneracy). Another is when multiple carrier pockets71,95,96 of one band in the BZ are degenerate as they are equivalent due to the crystal symmetry (valley degeneracy). One helpful way to increase the degeneracy of band and hence ZT is via converging different bands that were inherently not degenerate due to orbital or BZ symmetry. It is possible to make many valleys of a bulk material converge by tuning the doping and composition of the sample class. By this technique, Pei et al.78 achieved a convergence of at least 12 valleys in doped PbTe1−xSex alloys and resulted in an extraordinary ZT ∼ 1.8 at about 850 K, as shown in Fig. 7a. If two VBs [L(light) and Σ(heavy)] are present in a material, then reducing the energy offset between two VBs (ΔEL–Σ) can result in a remarkable effect of band convergence. Applying band engineering to converge the valence (or conduction) bands to achieve high valley degeneracy has become a general strategy in improving bulk thermoelectric materials because it simultaneously leads to a high Seebeck coefficient and high electrical conductivity. Fu et al.74 found that p-type Half-Heusler (HH) alloy Ti-doped FeV0.6Nb0.4 Sb of the composition Fe(V0.6Nb0.4)1−xTixSb (where, x = 0.04–0.24) with a high band degeneracy of NV = 8, can achieve a high ZT of ∼0.8 at 900 K for x = 0.2, which is one of the best-reported ZT values among the p-type HH compounds. The band effective mass of this system is found to be high  which leads to low mobility, but its low DP and low alloy scattering potential contribute to reasonably high mobility.
 Fig. 7  Band convergence and nestification. (a) Schematic diagram of temperature-induced band convergence in PbTe; (b) inherent band nestification in elemental Te, enabling a large number of effective band valley degeneracy. Reproduced from ref. 153. Copyright © 2017. (c) Schematic representation of band structure for SnTe and Mn-doped SnTe describing the synergistic band engineering. Copyright (2018) Wiley. Used with permission from ref. 97. 
According to the electronic structure calculations by Guo et al.,97 the addition of Mn increases Eg of SnTe and reduces the ΔEL–Σ. Due to the band convergence, heavy holes contribute more to electronic transport. As a result, the Seebeck coefficient and hence PF is improved Fig. 7c. Recently Luo et al.80 achieved a substantially improved TE performance in p-PbSe-based materials Ag doping coupled with SrSe or BaSe alloying. While 4d-states of Ag raise the energy of Σ-bands, Sr and Ba atoms hinder the contribution of 6s2-states of Pb and decrease the energy of the L-bands of p-PbSe. As a result ΔEL–Σ gets reduced remarkably, thereby onset of the VB convergence is shifted to lower temperatures (450 K) regime. Recently AgCl-doping and Sb-alloying have been proved to converge bands in SnTe effectively.81 Solubility of MnTe in SnTe indeed increases up to ∼25% with the help of 5% GeTe. An increase in solubility results in band convergence when MnTe concentration reaches ∼20%.75 Xie et al.98 alloyed GeTe and PbTe into SnTe. In this study, the origin of multiple electronic valleys in SnTe was explained using the “band inversion” principle, and the mechanism is schematically represented in Fig. 8a and b. GeTe or PbTe alloying separately can increase the number of VBs of SnTe, but GeTe/PbTe co-alloying in SnTe gives rise to at least three VBs that are almost converged in energy. Further doping with Cd-enlarges the band gap. A remarkably high ZT > 1.4 was achieved near 873 K in the Sn0.48Cd0.02Ge0.25Pb0.25Te sample.
 Fig. 8  (a) Schematic representation of the second electronic band in the vicinity of the primary one. Doping of guest elements effectively reduces energy separation of the two bands, (b) band inversion, the exchange of the conduction and VBs after band crossing. Reprinted from ref. 98. Copyright (2020), with permission from Elsevier. 
2.5 ‘Diffusion of carriers’ and ‘phonon-drag’
‘Diffusion of carriers’ and ‘phonon-drag’ both enhance a particular material's Seebeck coefficient. Over the past few decades, the ZT value of TE materials has been successfully enhanced above 300 K. Still, a relatively small Seebeck coefficient and high thermal conductivity at low temperatures resist that enhancement of ZT. ‘Phonon-drag’ is dominant at lower temperatures and is weak at higher carrier concentrations. In the expressions for the Seebeck coefficient, the effect of phonons flowing between the two ends is not considered. But the fact is, phonons travel from the hot side to the cold side due to the temperature gradient between the hot and cold ends. During this travel, phonons and charge carriers would interact, and part of the momentum from phonons will transfer to the charge carrier. The charge carriers would gain extra momentum from phonons in the forward direction and travel from the hot side to the cold side by contributing to extra current to be balanced out by the generated electric field and an increased Seebeck coefficient. Phonon-drag increases the effective mass of carriers (electrons or holes) due to interactions with the crystal lattice through which the carrier moves. This effect leads to a decrease in carrier mobility, increase in S and a reduced κ. Phonons contributing to phonon-drag are with longer mean free path and lower frequency. Still, phonons with a shorter mean free path and higher frequency contribute more to thermal conductivity than phonon-drag.7,33,56–61 The ‘Phonon-drag’ effect is significant only in materials where phonons are not strongly scattered and have long mean free paths and the strong electron–phonon interaction.99 Phonon-drag has a more significant contribution from the longitudinal phonon modes than the transverse modes due to the greater group velocities in the former mode. But overall, the diffusion of carriers is the most common process that contributes to the Seebeck coefficient of material and dominates at higher temperatures.56–61 First-principle computation of the phonon-drag effect was reported by Zhou et al.60 Fauziah et al.57 observed size dependency of the phonon-drag factor of the Seebeck coefficient due to enhancing the phonon-boundary scattering in micro/nanometer-scale Si wires. Applying linear response theory in the presence of impurity band, beyond Boltzmann transport regime, Matsuura et al.56 studied the effects of phonon-drag on resistivity and the Seebeck coefficient of the FeSb2 sample.
2.6 Reduction of dimensionality of the materials
The quantities in ZT expression are strongly correlated in 3D crystalline systems, making it difficult to control these variables independently to improve ZT. Energy dependence on DOS in different dimensions is depicted in Fig. 9.
 Fig. 9  Electronic DOS for (a) 3D-bulk, (b) 2D-quantum well, (c) 1D-nanowire or nanotube and (d) 0D-quantum dot. Copyright (2018) Wiley. Used with permission from ref. 18. 
If the dimensionality of the material is reduced and system size decreases up to the nanometer length scale, it becomes easier to manipulate the TE properties of materials. The quantum confinement effect on carriers led to the enhancement in power factor in low-dimensional systems.67,100,101 A similar spatial confinement effect on phonon was also observed and hence caused strong modification of κlattice by manipulating phonon group velocity. In addition to that, different kinds of interface scatterings can't be neglected when the dimension of materials is reduced.102 In 1993, Hicks and Dresselhaus proposed that the sharply peaked electronic density of states (DOS) in 2D quantum wells and 1D quantum wires effectively improve PF and ZT.103,104 This work motivated the development of 2D-“superlattice” structures to acquire high TE performance. The use of numerous interfaces (2D) is to scatter phonons more effectively than electrons and to scatter, preferably those phonons that contribute most strongly to the thermal conductivity, not those contributing to phonon-drag. For any material κlattice reaches its lowest value at the amorphous phase with the same chemical composition, which is the “amorphous limit”, but it is very challenging to reach that point or beyond it. “Superlattice” structures are specially designed to eliminate this problem and are usually layered phononic materials. Two strategies were found that reduce κlattice remarkably in low dimension materials. The first strategy is to impose a significant mass mismatch between two intercalated layers, and the second is the weakening of inter-layers interactions compared to those intra-layers.102,105–109 Therefore, ZT can be further enhanced by reducing lattice thermal conductivity in a wide variety of thermoelectric systems. SnTe/PbSe, SnTe/PbS, EuTe/PbTe superlattices, and SnEuTe films also show better thermoelectric properties than bulk SnTe.110 A recent investigation on 3D-GeSe and monolayered-GeSe showed that both structures are equally stable and can break the benchmark limit of ZT ≈ 1 at room temperature.111
2.7 Some other strategies to improve PF
Energy filtering83–89,112 and charge density wave formation86,87,113 are other strategies to increase the PF[S2σ]. Carriers with low kinetic energies get stopped by the energy barrier generated by introducing nanometric precipitates at nanoprecipitate phase boundaries, but high-energy electrons pass through them; this mechanism is named energy filtering. Kim et al.90 reported experimental evidence of such carrier energy filtering effect in Te-nanoparticle-dispersed Bi0.5Sb1.5Te3 films. They observed that S-increased significantly from 169 μV K−1 to 248 μV K−1 when Te-nanoparticles of 15 nm diameter were increased up to 15 vol%. Though this increment in S-was accompanied by a decrement in σ, the overall PF was improved by 30%. Charge density wave (CDW), caused by the local lattice distortion, is a low-dimensional transport phenomenon involving strong electron–phonon coupling and reduces lattice thermal conductivity by breaking the translational symmetry of lattices. This method was investigated on CuI-doped Bi2Te2.1Se0.9 compounds.87 Tian et al.112 reported, nanoscale/microscale Ag precipitates in Ag-plated SnTe bulk introduce Ag/SnTe interfaces to enhance the Seebeck coefficient via energy filtering effect.
3. Reduction of thermal conductivity (κ)
There is a lower limit of κlattice in bulk materials, the value of κlattice cannot be reduced below that limit. Most of the defects in lattice scatter charge carriers as well as phonons. Thus improvement of ZT depends on the ratio μ-decreases with decrease in mean free path of electron (charge carrier), i.e., defect in lattice reduces μ. On the other hand, κlattice also decreases with the introduction of defects and disorders in a lattice. If κlattice-reduces faster than μ-then the ratio will be increased, increasing the value of ZT.100 Over the past few decades, it has been challenging to achieve ZT > 1 because it is hard to reduce thermal conductivity below the “amorphous limit” or, in some cases, below the “alloy limit”.91,92κ-consists of two components as, κ = κel + κlattice. Where, κel is the electronic part of thermal conductivity, κel = LσT [Wiedemann–Franz Law]. In these expressions L-is Lorenz number, and can be expressed as20 (20)L-is a universal constant of value 2.44 × 10−8 WΩ K−2 for degenerate limit. For the non-degenerate limit L-converges to another constant value of 1.5 × 10−8 WΩ K−2 for acoustic phonon scattering. A new formula is applicable to calculate the Lorenz number, derived after taking the first-order correction to the degenerate limit. In this case,  in units of 10−8 WΩ K−2 for L, and μV K−1 for S.93,94 We can see that all these three quantities (S, σ, κel) depends on the carrier concentration of that material. σ and κel-both increase but S-decreases with increasing carrier concentration. This carrier concentration dependent quantities can be modified by chemical doping. The increment or decrement of charge carrier concentration depends on the type of dopant used, but the enhanced scattering between dopants and carriers can also reduce the mobility of the charge carriers, which in turn will reduce κel. Considering all such factors, it was observed that good TE materials have an ideal density of charge carriers in a range from 1019 cm−3 to 1021 cm−3 and fall under the range of typical carrier concentration of degenerate semiconductors. Heat can be transported through solids via different types of carriers like electrons, phonons, magnons, etc. As they are degenerate semiconductors for good TE materials, phonons play a significant role in heat conduction. In such types of semiconductors, the maximum proportion of thermal conductivity comes from the lattice component. If the component κlattice-can be reduced by some method, then overall κ value can also be reduced effectively. Over the past decades, so many methods have been invented to reduce the κlattice-component.  where CV-is the specific heat of the material, v-is the velocity of sound through the material, l-is the mean free path of phonon in the material lattice. Now, these three quantities of a TE material should be small to reduce κlattice and hence κ.
3.1 Reduction of specific heat (CV) of the material
According to classical statistics CV per atom is 3 kB for all solids at all temperatures (Dulong–Petit's law). But later it was concluded that CV approaches Dulong–Petit's limit in solids at high temperatures only. So specific heat is a built-in property of a material and is difficult to manipulate. But the incorporation of liquid nature in a crystalline solid is helpful to modify their CV. Liquids restrict the propagation of transverse mechanical waves, and thus contribution from transverse phonon mode to the overall specific heat gets reduced, thereby leading to a significant reduction in CV. Many elemental, as well as complex liquids with N-atoms, show that their CV, decreases with temperature, from about 3N kB at the melting point to (2–2.5)N kB at high temperature.114,115 Copper chalcogenide materials at high temperatures exhibit superionic cubic phases, in which Cu-atoms flow through the rigid framework of chalcogen atoms. Liquid-like behavior [“phonon-liquid electron-crystal” (PLEC)] of Cu-chalcogenides provided a route to reduce CV below Dulong–Petit's limit even at high temperature. Liu et al.8 achieved ZT ∼ 1.5 at 1000 K in the Cu2Se sample. In this case, Se-atoms form an FCC rigid sublattice and provide an effective pathway for charge carrier transport. On the other hand, Cu-ions' sublattices are highly disordered around the Se-sublattice. At high temperatures, Cu-ions can travel among the interstitial sites, exhibiting a liquid-like character and results κ < 0.1 Wm−1 K−1. He et al.9 reported that a reduction in CV of lattice vibrations is more prominent in Cu2S than in Cu2Se. As a result, κ < 0.6 Wm−1 K−1 in the whole temperature range and a maximum ZT ∼ 1.7 at 1000 K was achieved. ZT ∼ 1.1 at 1000 K was achieved in high-density Cu2Te samples synthesized via direct annealing without a sintering process.10 Reduction in lattice thermal conductivity was observed in other materials with liquid-like nature like Li0.09Cu1.9Se,11 Cu5FeS4,12 Cu7PSe6,13 CuCrSe2,14 Cu1.94Se0.5S0.5,15 AgCrSe2,116etc. Roychowdhury et al.117 reported that scattering of heat-carrying acoustic phonon modes by soft phonons causes ultralow κlattice ∼ 0.35 Wm−1 K−1 and ZT ∼ 1.6 at 670 K in AgCuTe. Hexagonal-to-rocksalt superionic transition in AgCuTe was observed in the temperature range 380–510 K.
3.2 Reduction of phonon relaxation time
According to the Drude theory, κlattice is expressed by eqn (21) (21)
So, another way to minimize κlattice of TE material is to minimize τ of phonon by increasing the scattering rate of phonons or anharmonicity in the material. There are many strategies to reduce κlattice by reducing τ. Some strategies are discussed here. To summarize the phonon scattering, it is usually emphasized to achieve a nearly full-spectrum scattering to minimize τ of phonon that usually requires a coexistence of multiple types of scattering mechanisms. It is therefore challenging to unequivocally distinguish how much each type of mechanism is contributing to the reduction of κlattice.

3.2.1 Increment in scattering rate of phonons. 
Any scattering mechanism that chooses phonons to scatter more effectively than charge carriers (electrons or holes) can increase the ratio of electrical to thermal conductivity. In that case, phonon mean free path gets reduced. Various types of phonon scattering are schematically shown in Fig. 10. Scattering of phonons by other phonons, lattice defects, charge carriers, or impurities reduce κlattice by reducing the mean free path of phonons. According to the Debye–Callaway model κlattice can be expressed in terms of combined relaxation time (τc) as,118 (22)
 Fig. 10  Schematic diagram illustrating various phonon scattering mechanisms within a thermoelectric material, along with electronic transport of hot and cold electrons. Copyright (2010) Wiley. Used with permission from ref. 16. 
To minimize κlattice, τc should also be minimized. Overall scattering rate (τc−1) can be expressed as the combination of all possible scattering mechanisms. τc−1 = τPD−1 + (τDC−1 + τDS−1) + τIN−1 + τU−1(23)
The aim is to scatter phonons more effectively to reduce κlattice. Every scattering process has unique ω and temperature dependence. Scattering by point defects (0D) shows τPD−1 ∝ ω4, scattering from dislocation cores (1D) shows τDC−1 ∝ ω3, for dislocation strains (1D) τDS−1 ∝ ω1, for interface scattering (2D) by nanostructures or precipitates τIN−1 ∝ ω0 and phonon–phonon Umklapp scattering shows τU−1 ∝ ω2.118 Phonon–phonon Umklapp scattering enables effective scattering of phonons with all frequencies. Point defects effectively scatter high-frequency phonons, dislocations effectively scatter mid-frequency phonons, and interfaces mostly scatter low-frequency phonons. Temperature also plays a vital role in various types of phonon scattering, as shown in Fig. 11. At low-temperature regime, grain boundary scattering, at mid-temperature regime electron–phonon and point defect scattering, and high-temperature regime Umklapp scattering become more effective phonon scattering mechanism.119
 Fig. 11  Dominant modes of the phonon scattering at different temperature range. Reprinted from ref. 119. Copyright (2019), with permission from Elsevier. 
3.2.1.1 Point defects. 
Point defects affect the transport properties of the crystal lattice at a length scale of interatomic distance. Point defects are mainly vacancies, interstitials, substitutions, etc. point defect scattering is the most effective scattering mechanism to reduce κlattice for TE materials by reducing τ of phonons. Point defect scattering arises from the combination of mass field fluctuations and strain field fluctuations between the host and impurity atoms. Other than inherent vacancies, there can be vacancies created intentionally by forming solid solutions with a solvent compound with a smaller cation-to-anion ratio than the matrix. The solid solution method can also generate interstitial atoms, with a solvent compound having a larger cation-to-anion ratio. Phonon scattering by interstitial defects results in very low κlattice in SnTe–Cu2Te solid solutions. Pei et al.120 reported κlattice ∼ 0.5 Wm−1 K−1 at 850 K due to stronger phonon scattering arising from high solubility and large mass and strain fluctuations by interstitial Cu atoms. Kihoi et al.48 reported κlattice ∼ 0.86 Wm−1 K−1 for the Sn0.85Mn0.09Sb0.035Bi0.025Te sample at 773 K. The reduced lattice thermal conductivity originated from enhanced phonon scattering by introducing point defects resulting from mass fluctuation in the multiple dopants, grain boundaries, embedded nanostructures in the grains, and strained Mn-rich nano-precipitates in the grain boundary. In the case of (SnTe)3−3x(In2Te3)x solid solutions, a high vacancy concentration emerges on the cation sites. The strong vacancy phonon scattering between Sn-atoms and lattice vacancies enables a significant suppression of phonon propagation when combined with the scattering from In-rich nano-precipitates.121 Enhancement of phonon scattering without any detrimental influence on electronic transport by Cu-interstitials causes low κlattice ∼ 0.4 Wm−1 K−1 in SnTe–GeTe–MnTe alloys.75
3.2.1.2 Line defects. 
The density and position of the dislocation in a material can affect phonon transport significantly. An increase in dislocation density in TE materials can be achieved by plastic deformation, liquid-phase sintering, clustering of vacancies or interstitials in the grain of a single-phase material, etc. Wu et al.122 explained the evolution and migration at Grain Boundaries in SnTe. Sn0.995In0.005Te samples were synthesized by three different methods [water quenched ingot as cast (IAC), hand-milled + spark plasma sintered pellet (HMS), and ball milled + spark plasma sintered pellet (BMS)]. Dislocations were observed to migrate from the interiors of the grains in the IAC sample and form arrays at grain boundaries in the HMS and BMS samples. Characterization and model simulation suggests that the in-grain dislocations have less impact on heat-carrying phonon scattering than dislocation arrays at the grain boundaries. The in-grain dislocation is more efficient than dislocation arrays at the grain boundaries for charge carrier scattering. Guo et al.97 synthesized SnTe–MnTe alloys by annealing and hot pressing. In this case, the high-density dislocation cores were observed in Sn0.94Mn0.09Te. High-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image and its inverse fast Fourier transform (IFFT) images show several dislocation cores inside. 3D strain clusters found in the material should be dislocation clusters associated with Mn-interstitial atoms, which induces clustered strain fields. Enhancement in the lattice strain causes a reduction in the κlattice. Dislocations-induced lattice strain can locally change the phonon frequencies and reduce the group velocity of phonons. Kim et al.123 achieved sufficiently low κlattice in Bi0.5Sb1.5Te3 sample by producing the periodic dislocations at low-energy grain boundaries. They synthesized the sample using a liquid-phase compaction process. But the synthesis technique was a bit different from the traditional liquid-phase sintering process and included applied pressure and transient flow of the liquid phase during compaction because traditional sintering techniques also accompany a reduction in s. Finally, they achieved κlattice ∼ 0.33 Wm−1 K−1 at 320 K.
3.2.1.3 Plane defects. 
Low-frequency phonons are in the target region of interface scattering. Since the heat-carrying phonons usually have low frequencies at low temperatures, so interface scattering is more effective for reducing κlattice at low temperatures. Dense interfaces can be created in a material by introducing nanoscale precipitates, nanocomposites or reducing the grain size of materials. GeTe/Bi2Te3 superlattice-like materials are promising systems where plane defects can successfully reduce κlattice. Lower value of κlattice ∼ 0.162 Wm−1 K−1 was achieved due to the effects of interfaces and Bi atoms in Bi2Te3.124 Alloying Bi2Te3 in GeTe can generate vast Ge-vacancies in the GeTe lattice.125 Through proper heat treatment, Ge-vacancies produce nanoscale van der Waals gaps, and these vacancy gaps induce nanoscale ferroelectric domains with 180° domain walls. Finally, a hierarchical “ferroelectric” domain structure arises that was found beneficial to reduce κlattice.125 Grain boundaries, nanoscale precipitates, stacking faults caused low κlattice in the Ge0.89Sb0.1In0.01Te sample.126 The presence of grain boundary and stacking faults play an essential role in the reduction of κlattice of Cd/Bi co-doped GeTe.127 Ball milling, melt spinning, spark plasma sintering can effectively decrease the grain size and increase the density of the interface. The presence of grain boundary and the Si/Ge dopants can effectively scatter the phonons and reduce κlatticein 2D-SnSe sample.225


3.2.2 Presence of large anharmonicity. 
Anharmonicity and strong phonon–phonon interactions (PPI's) are probable origins of low thermal conductivity in various classes of materials. Phonon–phonon scattering has a lesser negative impact on electron mobility than alloy or defect scattering of phonons. In a temperature range where heat is conducted by acoustic phonons mostly, and scattering mechanism is mainly the intrinsic phonon–phonon scattering, the lattice thermal conductivity κlattice is approximated in terms of Grüneisen parameter (γ), Young's modulus (Y), and Debye temperature (θD) as129–132 (24)where,  = mean atomic weight of all the constituent atoms, a3 = average volume occupied by one atom, ϑ = number of atoms in the unit primitive cell, A = constant, ρ = density of the material, and T = absolute temperature.

Y, θD, and γ are related to the average velocity of sound (va). Relation of va with different parameters in 3D-bulk materials can be extracted from the following relations. (25) (26) (27) (28) (29)Here, vs = shear sound velocity, vl = longitudinal sound velocity, vp = Poisson ratio.129–131,133,134 It can be concluded that low Young's modulus, low shear modulus (G), and large Grüneisen parameter imply strong anharmonicity, strong phonon–phonon interactions, and low lattice thermal conductivity in a material. Low thermal conductivity due to large anharmonicity has been observed in the case of IV–VI metal chalcogenides, as SnTe,135,136 layered orthorhombic SnSe,137–140etc. If a crystal is fabricated without any structural defects even then also κlattice has a finite value because of the anharmonic phonon scattering by the Umklapp process. Other than phonon–phonon Umklapp scattering, there are various other origins of anharmonicity in a lattice.
Bonding heterogeneity and lone-pair induced anharmonicity can cause ultralow thermal conductivity. During thermal vibration, interactions between nonbonding lone-pair electrons and bonding electrons of the neighboring atoms due to the overlapping wave functions occur. These overlaps induce nonlinear electrostatic repulsive forces, which resist lattice vibration. As a result, the outermost electron shell containing a lone pair of electrons gets deformed largely by lattice vibrations. Thus strong anharmonicity comes into play due to remarkable change in nonlinear terms in total energy associated with the crystal.131,140–143 Group-V elements As, Sb, Bi have s2-lone-pair. Stereo-chemically active lone-pairs can cause anharmonicity in lattice and lower the thermal conductivity of solids. Nielsen et al.142 theoretically and experimentally found that presence of group-V elements in the whole class of simple high-symmetry crystalline solids of composition ABX2 [A = Cu, Ag, Au, Na, K, Rb, Cs; B= As, Sb, Bi; and x= S, Se, Te] generates highly anharmonic acoustic phonons. The s-electron shell of the group-V elements can be easily deformed by lattice vibrations and leads to a strong anharmonicity. Anharmonicity in some of the large-grained polycrystals like AgSbSe2, AgBiSe2, Ag0.73Sb1.12Te2, NaSbSe2, NaBiTe2, and NaSbTe2 can be so strong that phonon scattering reduces κlattice to the amorphous limit. According to Skoug et al.,140 Cu3SbSe4, CuSbSe2, and Cu3SbSe3 exhibit different Se–Sb–Se angles. Mutual interaction between the 5s lone pair electrons of Sb and the adjacent valence electrons during thermal agitation leads to large lattice anharmonicity in the lattice. In the case of AgPbBiSe3, lone pairs of Bi and Pb-atoms strengthen their bonds with Se by off-centering and weaken Ag–Se bonds.144 A combination of chemical and structural heterogeneity arises at atomic scales that reduce τ of acoustic phonon scattering and hence reduce κlattice.144 Comparative study on κlattice of different Ga and In-monochalcogenides by Nissimagoudar et al.143 reveals that anharmonic parameters play a more vital role than harmonic parameters in these monolayers. As in this case, GaS (InS) has a higher κlattice than GaTe(InTe) and GaSe(InSe) it can be concludes that anharmonic scattering rate in GaS(InS) is lower than that in GaSe(InSe) and GaTe(InTe). But the actual picture turned out to be completely different. GaS, InS were found to have a higher anharmonic scattering rate than other group-13 monochalcogenides because large anharmonic interatomic force constants (IFCs) are associated with strong interaction between nonbonding lone-pair s electrons and bonding electrons of adjacent Ga/In atoms. In SnS0.5Se0.5, 5s2 lone-pairs of Sn are more localized than in SnS and strengthen the anisotropy of Fermi surface and lattice anharmonicity.145
Generally, strong anharmonicity is observed in materials with a distorted crystal structure. Still, there are so many highly symmetric crystal structures like SnTe, PbTe, Bi2Te3 (rock-salt structure), etc., that also exhibit strong anharmonicity.135,136,146 Structural instability, softening of transverse optical phonon modes, and long-range interatomic interaction due to the presence of resonant bonds contribute to large lattice anharmonicity and strong phonon–phonon scattering in materials having symmetric crystal structure.117,146–149 Long-range interaction along 〈100〉 direction was observed in rock-salt structured materials due to resonant bonding.136,150,151 The importance of this interaction decreases with increasing distortion of the crystal structures. Unlike Coulomb interaction, the strength of long-range interaction does not decrease monotonously with increasing atomic distance, and instead, the strength fluctuates in a pretty irregular manner. Long-range interaction induces near ferroelectric instability in IV–VI metal chalcogenides.136,152 Ferroelectric instability softens optical phonon modes. Soft phonon modes help to reduce κlattice by introducing strong anharmonic scattering and a large scattering phase space volume.151 Using inelastic neutron scattering (INS) and first-principles simulations, Li et al.149 confirmed strong anisotropic nature and giant anharmonicity of phonons in SnSe. They identified that strong anharmonicity in SnSe originates from a bonding instability from the long-range resonant p-bond network of Se-atoms, coupled to stereo-chemically active lone-pair of Sn atom.
Solids possess 3N vibrational modes, among which 3 modes are acoustic, and 3(N–1) are optical.115 The optical modes store most thermal energy, but they contribute little to heat conduction due to their low group velocity. In addition, the heat-carrying acoustic modes tend to interact with and are suppressed by the optical branches.154 An increase in ‘N’- means an increase in crystal's complexity and maybe a larger unit cell. So complex crystals increase the number of optical modes that, in turn, reduces κlattice. Phonon–phonon scattering is a consequence of the anharmonicity of the chemical bond. Acoustical to optical phonon scattering effectively can reduce κlattice. From the dispersion relation of optical and acoustic phonon branches, we notice a “frequency gap” between them at the BZ boundary, which reduces with a reduction in mass ratio between the constituent atoms of the unit cell, as shown in Fig. 12. That lattice restricts the propagation of any vibrational mode that falls within that gap. The extent of the frequency gap between the acoustic and optical branches regulates the Umklapp scattering rate. A small gap size corresponds to the high probability of longer wavelength phonons taking part in the Umklapp scattering process. That means a small frequency gap leads to higher group velocity (longer wavelength) phonons scattered into the optical phonons, and optical phonons have negligible impact on the thermal conduction as they have near-zero group velocity .155,156 From INS measurements and first-principles computations of the phonons, Delaire et al.146 identified strong anharmonic coupling between the transverse optic mode and the longitudinal acoustic modes of phonon in PbTe is the cause of its low thermal conductivity though having a simple rock-salt type crystal structure. According to Tian et al.,147 the softening of optical phonon modes plays an essential role in the thermal conduction of bulk PbSe and PbTe and serves as important scattering channels for acoustic phonons. In a recently reported work, Wu et al.157 theoretically showed that coupling of spin–orbit interaction with phonon anharmonicity has a significant effect on κlattice of SnSe.
 Fig. 12  Schematic diagrams showing the acoustical-to-optical phonon scattering process. The mass ratio of constituent atoms is related to the gap between the acoustic and optical branches. Reproduced from ref. 224 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry. 
Another source of anharmonicity is the presence of “Rattling modes” that were first found in materials with cage-like structures such as clathrates,158,159 skutterudites,160 and cobaltates.161 Materials with open crystallographic positions that can serve as an efficient “host” for inserted atoms are weakly bonded with the basic structural frame. As a result, they can vibrate around the central position (known as rattlers). In most of the bulk materials, the optical phonons have a lesser contribution in lattice thermal conductivity. When the system size reaches the nanoscale, the contribution of optical phonons in lattice thermal conductivity increases.162 But contribution in thermal conduction by acoustic phonon modes cannot be entirely excluded by introducing structural complexity only. Resonant phonon modes originated from “rattling” atoms, or atom clusters can reduce the velocity of the remaining acoustic phonons.154 In resonant bonding, coupling between optical and acoustic phonon modes happens mainly due to the softening of the optical branch, but according to the rattling model, both types of modes get softened.150 The Skutterudite system consists of voids in the structure. Filling those voids with the suitable type of atoms yields a remarkable reduction in the κlattice. Because atoms residing in the void space “rattle” there substantially affect the phonon propagation through the lattice.163 Clathrates emphasize the spatial separation of two substructures known as host clathrate framework and rattling guests. The host framework eases the transport of charge carriers and enhances PF, whereas the rattling of guests inside oversized cages of the host framework reduces κlattice by introducing scattering of acoustic phonons or reducing phonon group velocity.158,159 According to further research works, not only caged materials exhibit rattling modes. Without the presence of an oversized cage in LaOBiS2−xSex, “planar rattling” motions of Bi atoms can scatter phonon-like rattling in the case of caged compounds.164 NaxCoO2,161 CuP2,165 AgIn5S8,166 CsAg5Te3, AgBi3S5, BiCuSeO, Cu3SbSe3, Zintl tellurides such as TlInTe2 and InTe,117etc. also shows low κlattice originated from the presence of “rattling modes”. Jana et al.141 reported ultralow κlattice in mixed (In+/In3+) valent InTe. The rattling In+ cation with 5s2 lone-pairs leads to the generation of strongly anharmonic phonons, which couple with the heat-carrying acoustic phonon modes and lead to an ultralow κlattice.

3.3 Reduction of phonon group velocity
An effective strategy to reduce the κlattice, regardless of other scattering mechanisms present, is to slow down heat conduction by phonons lowering their group velocity. To discuss the strategies for reducing phonon group velocity, we take a simplified picture of a lattice. Where successive atoms with an average mass  is connected by a spring (chemical bonds) of length a and restoring force constant is K (strength of chemical bond), Phonon group velocity (v) is 115 The expression of phonon group velocity indicates that weak restoring force, as well as the heavy atomic mass of the constituent elements, are advantageous to reduce v. A more detailed discussion leads to the concept of low phonon group velocity or low sound velocity in materials with global or local soft bonds. Global soft bonds reduce group velocity overall, while local soft bonds induce low-frequency optical modes that interact locally with high-frequency acoustic modes.167 Ying et al.168 proposed α-MgAgSb as promising TE material in low-temperature range (300–550 K) and predicted coexistence of global and local weak chemical bonds to be the origin of the intrinsically low κlattice. In this case, Mg–Ag–Sb bonds in α-MgAgSb vibrate locally and induce low-frequency optical phonons, resulting in “rattling-like” thermal damping to further reduce the κlattice. Additionally, valence electron count (VEC) = 8 for α-MgAgSb causes its weak bonding nature. The low VEC directly leads to weak interatomic interactions and global chemical bonding. It also causes the transformation to the low symmetry phase, which possesses three-center bonds and weak local chemical bonding. Low phonon velocity ∼ 1921 m S−1 and low κlattice ∼ 0.6 Wm−1 K−1 at room temperature was reported for α-MgAgSb. In the case of clathrates, the rattling of guest atoms is the main reason for reducing κlattice. There are some features of clathrates that reduce κlattice, e.g., vacancy formation, formation of superstructures, mass alternation, etc. Vacancy formation within the clathrate framework makes it less rigid, leading to a reduction in phonon group velocity. The formation of superstructures gives rise to high unit volumes causes less coordinated vibrations of framework atoms. Mass alternation within guest substructure alters phonon mean free path without affecting individual rattling modes. Mainly, rattling modes lower phonon group velocity because of avoided crossing acoustic modes or resonant scattering of phonons.159 Such an avoided crossing effect has been observed in Ba8Ga16Ge30 clathrate, where the presence of guest Ba-atom in the cages largely reduces the group velocity of high-frequency acoustic phonons.169 GeTe/PbTe co-alloying leads to significant lattice softening by intense internal stains, resulting from high-density dislocations at the grain boundaries. Xie et al.98 measured sound velocities through SnTe, Sn0.8Ge0.1Pb0.1Te, Sn0.6Ge0.2Pb0.2Te and Sn0.5Ge0.25Pb0.25Te samples to be 1946 m S−1, 1828 m S−1, 1670 m S−1 and 1500 m S−1 respectively. Reduction in sound velocity with increasing proportion co-alloying agents is a sign of lattice softening. The lattice softening leads to a low lattice thermal conductivity. In/Mn co-doping softens chemical bonds in SnTe. Softened chemical bonds lead to lower κlattice. As a result of In/Mn co-doping, ZT = 1.03 was achieved in Sn0.89In0.01Mn0.1Te at 923 K.171 Se- substitution caused larger atomic mass and smaller force constants and, therefore, low phonon group velocity for SnS0.5Se0.5.145
3.4 Nanostructure engineering
Nanostructure engineering, such as introducing quantum dots, quantum wells, nanowires, nanotubes, and thin films, has been widely utilized in TE materials to reduce the κlattice. Nanostructures can scatter medium-wavelength phonons and improve TE properties. Nanostructuring in bulk materials was first implemented in AgPbmSbTe2+m (LAST-m) system.17κlattice of PbS can be significantly reduced by introducing nanostructured second phases. Doping with SrS and CaS form a mixture of a nanostructured/solid solution material. Nanoscale precipitates and point defects both play an important role in reducing the κlattice.172 When the nanoprecipitates are coherently embedded in the matrix, it is called endotaxial nanostructures. Tan et al.208 reported that endotaxial CdS or ZnS nanoscale precipitates reduce κlattice of SnCd0.03Te remarkably with no effect on the PF. Sootsman et al.173 prepared bulk PbTe with nano inclusions of Sb, Bi, and InSb embedded in the matrix, which led to remarkably reduced κlattice. Low κlattice ∼ 1.1 Wm−1 K−1 due to collective phonon scattering from mesostructured grain boundaries, nanostructured precipitates, nanoscale defect layers, and solid solution point defects was achieved in Ge0.90Bi0.10Te.174 Nanostructured In-doped SnTe,175 nanostructured p-type Gex(SnyPb1−y)1−xTe alloys,176 (GeTe)75(AgSbTe2)y(AgSbSe2)x,177 TAGS-materials,178 solid solution series of Ge12In2Te15 and Ge12Sb2Te15 also show promising TE performance due to various nanostructuring.179
3.5 All-scale hierarchical architectures
The periodic arrangement of atoms in crystalline lattice results in periodic dependence of frequency ω() on the wave vector . Different types of waves of atoms' displacements can propagate in crystals, which can differ in the motions of atoms within one crystal cell. Consequently, several types of phonons in crystals are characterized by a specific dependence of energy on momentum. Recently, hierarchical phonon scattering has been suggestive of being effective in reducing the κlattice and hence, enhancing ZT of heavy-band TE materials.180 Hierarchical phonon scattering centers scatter the phonons with different frequencies. The hierarchical scattering centers should be at a shorter distance than the phonon mean free path (lph) but at a longer distance than the carrier mean free path (lc) so that phonons are more strongly scattered than charge carriers. The sub-microscale grain boundaries usually target low-frequency phonons, while more atomic-scale point defects can be designed to further enhance the scattering of high-frequency phonons. In Fig. 13, we schematically represent the hierarchical phonon scattering. Thus, by a suitable combination of the sub-microscale grain boundaries, atomic-scale point defects, and electron–phonon interaction, hierarchical phonon scattering centers can be concurrently created in a given material, resulting in a significantly reduced κlattice.
 Fig. 13  (a) A schematic sketch of hierarchical phonon scattering by the sub-microscale grain boundaries, point defects, and electrons. Reproduced from ref. 180. (b) Maximum achievable ZT values and scattering of phonon with different frequencies for the respective length scales. Reproduced from ref. 181. 
Biswas et al.181 achieved ZT ∼ 2.2 at 915 K in the PbTe–4 mol% SrTe–2 mol% Na sample, synthesized by powder processing and SPS method. Phonon scatterings from atomic-scale (alloy scattering), nanoscale (scattering from endotaxial precipitation), and mesoscale (scattering from grain boundaries) were introduced in the system. The origin of ultra-low κlattice in the case of In/Cu co-doped SnTe was explained by Guo et al.182 At room temperatures, Cu2Te precipitates are in the range of hundreds of nanometers to several micrometers in size, and the phonon scattering caused by these precipitates is minimal. As temperature increases, the gradual dissolution of Cu2Te precipitates creates more point defects and nanoprecipitates of several hundred nanometers to tens of nanometers, which strongly scatters the phonons. As a result κlattice ∼ 0.47 Wm−1 K−1 at 873 K was achieved in (SnTe)2.94(In2Te3)0.02–(Cu2Te)0.18. A reduced κlattice ∼ 0.4 Wm−1 K−2 was achieved by Xin et al.183 in Sn0.98V0.02Te. The hierarchical crystal defects and microstructures intensively scattered phonons and resulted in ultra-low κlattice. Wu et al.184 reported that doping Mn with SnTe induces nanoscale precipitates, laminates, stacking faults, and layers, which effectively scatter phonons on multiple length scales, leading to very low lattice thermal conductivities. Wang et al.185 achieved Ultralow κlattice in (Sn0.985In0.015Te)0.90(AgCl)0.1 by using this strategy. Low κlattice ∼ 1.1 Wm−1 K−2 due to collective phonon scattering from mesostructured grain boundaries, nanostructured precipitates, nanostructured defect layers, and solid solution point defects for Ge0.90Bi0.10Te was reported by Perumal et al.174
3.6 Reduction of bipolar thermal conduction
There is another component of κ-which is present in most of the narrow band gap semiconductors (or semimetal or wide band gap semiconductor at elevated temperature) and is denoted by κbipolar, which can be treated as a part of κel. Thermal excitation leads the electrons to move from VB to CB, generating two types of charge carriers (electrons and holes). Due to the thermal excitation, there is almost no change in the number of majority carriers, but the number of minority carriers changes significantly. At this point, two oppositely charged carriers take part in heat and electricity conduction; this phenomenon is called the “Bipolar effect”. This effect shows some negative impacts on the performance of TE materials. Heat is taken away by both types of carriers from the hot side to the cold side. Still, net current is zero as the direction of current flow is opposite to each other. The net Seebeck coefficient is reduced as oppositely charged carriers take part in the conduction process. In this case, Lorenz number can be expressed as32,33,120,129,186,187 (30)And the thermal conductivity for charge carriers can be expressed as (31)
So the fact is, electronic thermal conductivity is not the direct summation of the individual contributions from the two bands (κe, κh). The additional term arises because electrons and holes can move together in the same direction to transport energy, but no net charge is being carried. To achieve better thermoelectric efficiency, this additional component should be reduced. This additional term, κbipolar-is expressed as [subscript ‘e’ and ‘h’ stand for electrons and holes respectively]. (32)
Total electrical conductivity (σ) = σe + σh. It is easy to see that the bipolar contribution is essential only when σe ≈ σh  Say, ηe = η and ηh = −(ηG + η)where, ηG = reduced band gap = η = reduced Fermi energy = ηe-is reduced Fermi energy measured from the CB extremum and, ηh = reduced Fermi energy measured from the VB extremum,188 (33)r-is the scattering parameter, and γ-is a material dependent parameter called the “Grüneisen parameter”. Bipolar heat conduction is usually observed in narrow band gap (Eg ≲ 1 eV) semiconductors where intrinsic electrical conductivity is comparatively larger, and the electronic part of thermal conductivity is more prominent than the lattice part of thermal conductivity. From eqn (33) We can conclude that κbipolar-can be reduced if scattering parameter(r) is decreased, band gap (Eg) is increased, the ratios  are increased, the carrier concentration is increased and hence EF is increased. As the temperature increases, these parameters changes in such a way that κbipolar-increases. Nanoinclusions can reduce bipolar thermal conduction.166 Kim et al. reported that In-doping reduces the bipolar thermal conduction due to the increased Eg of Bi0.4Sb1.6Te3.189 Doping with Mn also helps to reduce bipolar conduction in SnTe.184
4. Recent strategies to search for highly efficient TE materials
A simple model based on effective mass approximation predicts that inelastic scattering in 2D semiconducting materials with step-like DOS can ensure a high power factor.190 The inelastic processes delay the onset of scattering and maximize the utilization of the Fermi window for transport. The simulations showed that the materials with dominant inelastic processes, specifically when optical phonon energy Eph ∼ 5 kBT, improve the power factor. Thus, the material selection rules formulated in such a manner may trigger the researchers to predict and preselect 2D materials, thereby reducing the time and resources in the involved processes. Another crucial factor is atomic disorder, which is present in most high-performance TE materials, and plays an essential role in modulating electronic and thermal transport. The disorder-induced electronic localization has contradictory behavior on σ and S. As the localization increases with increasing disorder, σ-decreases, whereas S-increases Fig. 14. Effectively tuning the disorders can lead to a high PF. Recently, Roychowdhury et al.191 have established the positive effect of the disorder tuning on electronic and phonon transports, simultaneously optimized toward high thermoelectric performance in Cd-doped polycrystalline AgSbTe2. Doing Cd in AgSbTe2 enhances cationic ordering mainly. Enhanced cationic ordering improves electronic properties due to increased localization of electronic states and reduces κlattice by introducing nanoscale superstructures and coupling soft vibrations localized within nanometer range around dopant sites with local strain modulation. They finally achieved ZT ∼ 1.5 near 300 K and a maximum ZT ∼ 2.6 at 573 K in 6 mol% Cd-doped AgSbTe2. Tang et al.192 reported remarkable improvement in TE performance of Sn1/3Pb1/3Ge1/3Te solid solution due to a reduction in κlattice caused by massive disordered cations in this system. The cationic disorder introduced significant mass/strain contrasts for broadening the phonon dispersion and low κlattice was achieved for the entire temperature range. Organic–inorganic composite materials are also promising for TE application. Ju et al.193 prepared SnSe Nanosheets (NSs) by hydrothermal lithium intercalation combined with an exfoliation process from SnSe ingots and then fabricated SnSe NS/PEDOT: PSS composites. ZT reached the maximum value of ∼0.32 when the content of SnSe NS reached 20 wt%.
 Fig. 14  A schematic of disorder-tuning of mobility edge (Ec) separating the localized electronic states from the extended ones and how it affects the electrical conductivity (σ) and Seebeck coefficient (S). EF denotes the Fermi energy. The horizontal red arrow denotes the shift in Ec toward the Fermi energy EF with increasing disorder. Reproduced from ref. 191. 
5. Sn chalcogenides as TE materials
The 16th group elements in the periodic table are usually known as chalcogens. Compounds containing a chalcogen element as an anion and any metal as a cation are commonly called metal chalcogenides. In this review, we will discuss the improvement of TE performance of Tin chalcogenides (SnTe, SnSe, SnS) and Germanium chalcogenides (GeTe, GeSe, GeS) due to the application of various traditional and novel strategies that we have discussed in previous sections. This section has explicitly discussed the progress of Sn-chalcogenide-based materials for TE application in the past years.
5.1 SnTe-based materials

5.1.1 Crystal structure and general transport properties of SnTe. 
SnTe is an extrinsic (p-type) semiconductor. Among chalcogenides of Sn, only SnTe has a rocksalt crystal structure (Fmm) and isotropic thermoelectric properties.194,195 The rocksalt crystal structure of SnTe is shown in Fig. 15a.
