SnTe has a ferroelectric rhombohedral (R3m) phase which transit to the cubic (Fmm) phase at a carrier concentration-dependent transition temperature, roughly ∼100 K.194 Knox et al.196 reported that SnTe exhibits a crossover to an “emphanitic phase” above 300 K where the sample becomes locally distorted (Sn-off centering), keeping the global symmetry as cubic. SnTe has topological crystalline insulating properties.197–199 Two alternative models have been proposed for the band structure of SnTe.170 The first one is the two-valence-band model (suggested by Allgaier and Scheie).,200 and the second one is the semimetal model (proposed by Sagar and Miller).201 SnTe has a high concentration of Sn-vacancies, and those vacancies act as acceptors. The carrier concentration normally ranges from 1020 cm−3 to 1022 cm−3.202,203 Because of the high concentrations of Sn vacancies, phonons are scattered both by three-phonon Umklapp processes and by the Sn vacancies, this causes κlattice-to vary with temperature as T−1/2 instead of T−1.202 High carrier concentration results in high κlattice ∼ 2.88 Wm−1 K−1 but ultimately Seebeck coefficient reduces and results in a poor ZT (∼0.5 at 873 K).184 At room temperature S-does not decrease with an increase in hole concentration (n) as it generally should be. Instead, it increases from 5–8 μV deg−1 at n = 1–2 × 1020 cm−3 to a maximum of 34 μV deg−1 at n = 8 × 1020 cm−3, after which it decreases to 20.5 μV deg−1 at n = 1.8 × 1021 cm−3 and the maximum value gradually disappears with increasing temperature. At comparatively higher temperature (673–723 K) S-decreases gradually with increasing hole concentration, due to the presence of two nondegenerate VBs [light VB (L) and heavy VB (Σ)].202 The direct band gap is tiny (0.18 eV at room temperature), as shown in Fig. 15b, which leads to a significant Bipolar transport of carriers. The energy difference between light and heavy VB is ∼0.3 eV at room temperature, resisting the heavier holes to contribute to the Seebeck coefficient.195 Under ambient conditions, n-type SnTe has better S-and ZT-than p-type SnTe. An increment in external pressure on n-type SnTe causes an enhancement of its TE performance up to a maximum of 5 GPa then degrades afterward.204 Peng et al. reported the synthesis of n-type SnTe through Pb/I doping. Pb-helped to suppress the high vacancy concentration of SnTe and its electrical transport property was optimized by I-doping.203 Ferroelectricity arises in non-centrosymmetric crystal structures. A local ferromagnetic ordering has been reported above room temperature in rock salt SnTe (centrosymmetric).205 It was concluded that there are two reasons behind this anomaly. One is the local structural distortion due to Sn-off centering, and another is due to the existence of rhombohedral strain in the cubic phase of SnTe. Chang et al.206 thinned SnTe up to 1 unit cell and found a surprising rise in the ferroelectric transition temperature from <100 K in bulk SnTe to 270 K in this ultrathin SnTe film.
 Fig. 15  (a) Crystal structure of cubic SnTe (a = 6.313 Å). (b) Schematic diagram of SnTe bands at 573 K. Reproduced from ref. 195. 


5.1.2 TE performance of SnTe based materials. 
The presence of Sn-vacancies causes extremely high carrier (hole) concentration in SnTe, making it a poor TE material with a low Seebeck coefficient and high κel. Introduction of multiscale scattering centers, including atomic-scale point defects (0D), nanoscale endotaxial precipitates, mesoscale grain boundaries, dislocations (1D), nanostructures, and other recently proven strategies of band engineering, including band convergence, resonant level generation, etc. can enhance ZT in SnTe effectively. The maximum value of ZT achieved for different SnTe based materials due to various strategies is schematically represented in Fig. 16 to provide a comparative overview.
 Fig. 16  The highest achieved figure of merit (ZTmax) for different compositions of SnTe-based materials [data taken from Table 2]. 
Hg alloying of SnTe effectively suppressed the bipolar thermal conduction by increasing the band gap; this results in high ZT ∼ 1.35 at 910 K for 2% Bi-doped SnTe with 3% HgTe (Sn0.98 Bi0.02Te–3% HgTe). Here Bi acts as an electron donor dopant.207 As already discussed, stoichiometric SnTe is not a very good thermoelectric material due to its extremely high hole concentration arising from intrinsic crystal defects. One of the strategies to improve the TE performance of SnTe is to reduce its carrier concentration. Tan et al.208 reported that Sn self-compensation could reduce the Sn-vacancies and remarkably decrease the hole density. 3 mol% self-compensation of Sn-resulted in a maximum ZT ∼ 0.6 at 823 K. The solubility limit of Cd in SnTe is around 3% near room temperature. The p-type samples of SnCd0.03Te synthesized by a melting combined with spark plasma sintering (SPS) technique exhibit ZT ∼ 0.96 at 823 K, which is 60% improved than the Cd-free sample. Cd-dopants increase the band gap and reduce the energy offset between light and heavy VBs. Endotaxial CdS or ZnS-nanoscale precipitates reduced the κlattice of SnCd0.03Te without affecting the PF, and a maximum ZT ∼ 1.3 for CdS and ZT ∼ 1.1 for ∼ZnS at 873 K respectively was achieved.208 However, Hg can produce VB degeneracy more effectively than Cd. The class Sn1−xHgxTe exhibits lower lattice thermal conductivities than the same class replacing Hg by Cd. He et al.209 synthesized a series of high quality SnCdxTe (x = 0–0.15) by the zone-melting method. κlattice-reduces with the increase in Cd content and PF ∼ 25 μW cm−1 K−2 and ZT1.03 at 820 K was achieved in SnCd0.12Te. The solubility of CdSe in SnTe increases with increasing temperature. Heavy CdSe-alloying with SnTe causes the convergence of heavy and light VBs, which helps to improve PF. On the other hand, cation–anion point defects and boundary interfaces of CdSe precipitates cause strong phonon scattering and result in a low κlattice ∼ 0.8 Wm−1 K−1 As a result, a high peak ZT ∼ 1.1 is achieved in (SnTe)0.90(CdSe)0.10 at 850 K due to the synergistic effect of improvement in electronic and thermal performances, which demonstrates (SnTe)1−x(CdSe)x as promising TE materials.210
Dopants like, Mn, Sb, Bi,211 In, Mg,212 Zn,213 Ca,214 V,183 I, Gd, and Ag improves TE performance SnTe. He et al.215 reported that alloying with Mn can improve the Seebeck coefficient by suitably modulating the band structure of SnTe. Their work reported a maximum ZT ∼ 1.25 at 920 K for p-type SnMn0.07Te sample synthesized by zone melting method. Sn self-compensation and Mn alloying improved the ZT of SnTe significantly. This process reduces the carrier concentration of SnTe and increases the Seebeck coefficient. Sn1.03−xMnxTe (with, x = 0, 0.05, 0.07, 0.09, 0.11) class of materials was studied over a temperature range of 300 K to 900 K, and an enhanced ZT ∼ 1.35 was reported for the composition of Sn0.94Mn0.09Te at 873 K.97 Zhou et al.216 synthesized Mn-substituted SnTe and SnO2 nanoinclusions by in situ chemical reactions between the MnO2 additives and SnTe matrix. They achieved a ZT of ∼1.5 at 873 K in the SnTe + 10 mol% MnO2 sample. Tan et al.217 investigated the effect of alloying the Sn-sites in SnTe with Mn using experimental and computational methods. Mn solubility is relatively higher in SnTe (>9%) than that of Cd and Hg. Mn dopant decreases the energy separation between the light and heavy VBs and increases the band gap of SnTe. When the Mn content in SnTe exceeds its solubility limit, MnTe nanoprecipitates are formed. MnTe nanoprecipitates restrict the phonon propagation and reduce the κlattice. From the study on the class of materials Sn1−xMnxTe (x = 0 to 50 mol%), the highest ZT ∼ 1.3 at 900 K was observed for x = 12 mol%. Another work by Wu et al.184 estimated the solubility of Mn in SnTe to be around 13%, and they reported the highest ZT ∼ 1.3 at 900 K for 17% Mn alloyed with SnTe. ZTavg ∼ 0.5 in a temperature range of 600 K to 900 K was achieved in this case. MnTe is a good band convergent to improve the TE performance of SnTe though its solubility is only about 15 at% in SnTe. Additional alloying of SnTe–MnTe with PbTe (i.e., Sn0.8−xMn0.2PbxTe) increases the solubility of MnTe in SnTe and also manipulates different thermoelectric properties of SnTe and results in a very high ZT ∼ 1.5 in SnTe with a 20 at% MnTe and a 30 at% PbTe alloying (Sn0.5Mn0.2Pb0.3Te).218 Solubility of MnTe in SnTe can also be increased up to ∼25% by alloying with 5% GeTe. Further alloying with Cu2Te enabled a sufficient reduction in κlattice to its amorphous limit and ZT ∼ 1.8 above 600 K.75 Sb and Bi act as donors and reduce the Hall carrier concentration and improve TE performance. Sb-is an effective dopant to improve the TE performance of SnTe. Banik et al.219 achieved ZT ∼ 1 for the composition of Sn0.85Sb0.15Te at 800 K. Sb-optimized the hole concentration of SnTe and formed endotaxial Sb-rich nanoprecipitates (2–10 nm) and super-structured intergrowth nano-domains (10–30 nm), which led to a better TE performance by reducing thermal conductivity. Taking Sn0.85Sb0.15Te as the matrix, Fu et al.220 reported that Sb/Mg-alloying resulted in a maximum ZT ∼ 1.02 at 773 K for Sn0.79Sb0.15Mg0.06Te. Comparatively, light element Mg introduces large mass and strain fluctuations in the matrix and also increases band degeneracy. As a result κlattice gets reduced and S enhanced, leading to a better TE performance. Studies of Sn1−xSb2xTe1+2x materials synthesized by melt spinning and SPS technique were done by Yang et al.221 An ultralow κlattice of ∼0.57 Wm−1 K−1 at 773 K and the maximum PF of 24.2 μW cm−1 K−2 at 823 K for Sn0.91Sb0.18Te1.18 (x = 0.09) was achieved. For the same sample, ZT ∼ 0.95 at 823 K was reported, which is 2.2 times higher than that of pure SnTe alloy. Recently AgCl-doping and Sb alloying have been proved to improve the TE performance of SnTe. AgCl-introduces band convergence and increases band gap simultaneously while Sb-optimizes carrier concentration and reduces κlattice. Many properties of SnTe get modified when doped with Bi-to achieve better TE performance. Maximum ZT ∼ 0.87 at 773 K was achieved in (Sn0.81Sb0.19Te)0.93(AgCl)0.07.81 Zhou et al.211 observed reduction in carrier concentration, increase in DOS effective mass, and scattering phonons in a wide frequency range due to Bi-doping. Combining all the advantages, they achieved ZT ∼ 1.1 at 873 K in the Sn0.94Bi0.06Te sample. Studies of the TE properties of nanostructured SnTe with different dopants revealed that In-acts as a remarkably effective dopant to improve the TE performance of SnTe. Zhang et al.175 synthesized In-doped samples of SnTe by ball milling and hot pressing. Doping with In-enhances Seebeck coefficient by introducing resonant states near Fermi level and reduces κlattice by increasing the density of grain boundaries. A minimal amount of In (0.25 atom%) increased the ZT to ∼1.1 at 873 K. Moshwan et al.135 prepared In-doped SnTe samples by facile solvothermal method. ZT ∼ 0.78 and PF ∼ 21.8 μW cm−1 K−2 for Sn0.99In0.01Te was achieved at 773 K. An increase in Seebeck coefficient and reduction in κlattice were observed. Nanoprecipitates and In-rich fcc structured InTe precipitates in the SnTe matrix generate severe strain in the SnTe matrix. In addition to those point defects, dislocations and anharmonicity also cause the significant scattering of heat-carrying phonons resulting in a low κlattice. ZT ∼ 1.2 at 860 K was achieved in a crystalline ingot of p-type Sn0.94Mg0.09Te fabricated by sealed tube melting reactions.212 Zn acts as a resonant dopant for SnTe. For Zn doping, a highest ZT ∼ 1.49 at 840 K, a ZTavg ∼ 0.78 in the range 300–840 K, and ∼1.04 in between 500–840 K was achieved in the Sn0.96Zn0.08Te sample.213 Ca-alloying increases the band gap of SnTe, which restricts bipolar transport, reduces energy difference between heavy and light VB, enhances band convergence and effective mass. Ca-alloying with SnTe results ZT ∼ 1.35 at 873 K, ZTavg ∼ 0.77 in the range 300–900 K, and ∼0.93 in the range 500–900 K in Sn0.94Ca0.09Te.214 A reduced κlattice ∼ 0.4 Wm−1 K−2, an enhanced ZT ∼ 1.3 at 873 K and ZTavg ∼ 0.75 in the temperature range 623–873 K was achieved by Xin et al.183 in Sn0.98V0.02Te. The hierarchical crystal defects and microstructures intensively scattered phonons and resulted in ultra-low κlattice. Zhou et al.222 doped SnTe with I, Gd, and excess Te. They synthesized SnTe1−xIx (0 ≤ x ≤ 0.02), GdySn1−yTe (0 ≤ y ≤ 0.02) and SnTe1+z (0 ≤ Z ≤ 0.015) by melt alloying and hot pressing method. I, Gd, and Te modified its carrier concentration to achieve better TE performance. For SnTe0.985I0.015 sample ZT ∼ 0.6 at 700 K, ZTavg ∼ 0.35 in 300–773 K range along with ZT ∼ 0.4 at 773 K for Gd0.01Sn0.99Te and SnTe1.015 samples was reported in this work. Zhang et al.223 reported the highest ZT ∼ 1.1 at 873 K in the 11 atom% Ag-doped Gd0.06Sn0.94Te sample. Gd-introduces nano/mesoscale defects in the SnTe matrix, and Ag-improves the PF by optimizing carrier concentration.
Co-doping by two or more different dopants with SnTe is an effective way to enhance the TE performance of pristine SnTe. Co-doping shows synergistic effects to improve the TE performance of SnTe. In the case of In/Mn co-doping, an enhanced Seebeck coefficient of 116 μV K−1 at 300 K and a maximum ZT ∼ 1.15 at 823 K were observed in Sn0.915Mn0.11In0.005Te. The PF improves with an increase in the In-content for Sn0.92−xMn0.11InxTe class of samples. An increase in PF from 7.44 to 8.68 μW cm−1 K−2 for change in x from 0 to 0.0005 was observed at room temperature.225 Tan et al.226 reported that In/Cd co-doping and introducing nanoscale precipitations by CdS nanostructuring could improve the ZT value of SnTe. In-dopants modify the band structure by creating the resonance energy levels at the VB at low temperatures. Cd-dopants increase the band gap and make light and heavy hole VBs of SnTe converge at mid-temperature and high-temperature regimes. CdS significantly improves phonon scattering leading towards a low κlattice. A maximum ZT ∼ 1.4 at 923 K was achieved for Sn0.97In0.015Cd0.015Te nanostructured with 3% CdS. Moshwan et al.227 synthesized Sn(CdIn)xTe1+2x microcrystals by a facile solvothermal method. The presence of point defects, nanoprecipitates, lattice distortion, and grain boundaries in the system reduces κlattice significantly. A low κlattice ∼ 1.84 Wm−1 K−1, high PF ∼ 26.76 μW cm−1 K−2, and maximum ZT ∼ 1.12 (for x = 0.03) at 773 K was achieved. Sn1.04−2xMgxInxTe materials were synthesized by co-doping In/Mg with SnTe using modified self-propagating high-temperature synthesis (SHS) and spark plasma sintering (SPS) method. A high PF ∼ 42 μW cm−1 K−2 and a high ZT ∼ 1.5 at 840 K for x = 0.03 make it a very efficient material for thermoelectric applications.228 As an effect of co-doping of SnTe with In/Ca, the TE performance of SnTe was improved. The resonant levels introduced by In, the band gap opening by Ca, the VB convergence induced by Ca and In-synergistically increase the Seebeck coefficient for a wide range of temperatures. The ZT values in the case of Sn1.04−3xCa2xInxTe fabricated by SHS and direct current sintering (DCS), increases with an increase in doping concentration (from x = 0.00 to x = 0.04). High PF ∼ 47 μW cm−1 K−2, maximum ZT ∼ 1.65 at 840 K and a ZTavg ∼ 0.87 considering 500 K and 840 K as cold and hot end temperatures respectively were obtained for x = 0.04.229 Co-doping by In/Sr has been reported to increase the TE performance of pristine SnTe effectively. Sn1−3xInxSr2xTe samples were prepared by a facile solvothermal method. A low κlattice ∼ 0.57 Wm−1 K−1, PF ∼ 31.88 μW cm−1 K−2, and ZT ∼ 1.31 at 823 K were obtained for x = 0.025 later, and two quantities were respectively 121% and 364% higher than that of the undoped SnTe.230 High-level In and Ag co-dopants can remarkably converge two VBs and increase the density of states near the Fermi level, and this leads to an enhanced Seebeck coefficient from 95 μV K−1 in the pristine SnTe to 178 μV K−1 and ZT ∼ .38 at 823 K in Sn0.85In0.05Ag0.10Te sample fabricated by facile solvothermal method.231 For the class of samples, SnAgxInxTe1+2x Banik et al.232 reported a maximum ZT ∼ 1 and a significantly large thermoelectric PF ∼ 31.4 μW cm−1 K−2 for x = 0.025 at 856 K. In/Cu co-doping can enhance PF of SnTe and reduce κlattice as well. Guo et al.182 reported that In-dopant induces resonant level and multiple scales of Cu2Te nanoprecipitates, and Cu interstitials enhance phonon scattering. As a result, PF ∼ 29 μW cm−1 K−2, and ZT ∼ 1.55 at 873 K was achieved in (SnTe)2.94(In2Te3)0.02–(Cu2Te)0.18. In recent work, Wang et al.185 have shown that doping with In with pristine SnTe introduces resonance level, enhancing electrical transport properties. Further (Sn0.985In0.015Te)1−x(AgCl)x samples (with, x = 0, 0.05, 0.10, and 0.15) were synthesized via facile microwave wet-chemical method and SPS. The addition of AgCl introduces different hierarchical structures like grain boundaries, nanopores, nanoprecipitates, and dislocations. As a result, phonon scattering increases remarkably and κlattice is reduced gradually from pristine SnTe to Sn0.985In0.015Te and then to (Sn0.985In0.015Te)1−x(AgCl)x. For (Sn0.985In0.015Te)0.90(AgCl)0.10 sample κlattice ∼0.245 Wm−1 K−1 was recorded, breaking the “amorphous limit” and achieving ZT ∼ 0.86 at 823 K. Lu et al.233 reported the result of In/Pb co-doping with SnTe. Sn0.99−xPb0.01InxTe (x = 0.01, 0.02, 0.03) samples were synthesized by facile hydrothermal method. ZT ∼ 0.7 at 873 K in Sn0.96Pb0.01In0.03Te was achieved. Roychowdhury et al.234 worked on Sn0.70Pb0.30Te sample and improved its TE performance via Cd-and In-doping. Doping with Pb-synergistically enhances TE properties of SnTe by forming resonant levels and effectively converging VBs. Sn0.70Pb0.30Te-holds better TE properties than pristine SnTe. Further doping with 3% Cd-and 0.25% In-leads to a maximum ZT ∼ 0.82 at 654 K where Cd-and In-acts as a VB convergent and a resonant dopant, respectively. In/Hg co-doped SnTe has promising TE properties. The interplay between band convergence and resonant levels can be optimized for the PF of the system. For the Sn0.98Bi0.02Te–1% HgInTe2 sample, the peak ZT ∼ 0.9 was achieved at 850 K.235 Guo et al.236 improved the TE performance of SnTe by co-doping with Ce and Li. On the Sn0.98Ce0.02Te matrix, the content of Li2Te was adjusted, and ZT ∼ 1 at 873 K and ZTavg ∼ 0.39 from 300 K to 873 K was achieved in Sn0.98Ce0.02Te-(Li2Te)0.01 Sample. Tian et al.112 fabricated Ag-plated SnTe bulk using facile chemical electroless plating and SPS methods. They achieved ZT ∼ 0.67 at 823 K for 2 wt% Ag-plated SnTe. Partially doped Ag-atoms reduce Sn-vacancy concentration. Nano/microscale Ag-precipitates form Ag/SnTe interfaces, those act as energy filtering barriers to filter the negative contribution of low-energy carriers to the Seebeck coefficient. Another way to optimize the thermoelectric performance of SnTe is to make a composite of SnTe with a compound that holds the counter thermoelectric properties as of SnTe, and one such compound is CuInTe2. The most optimized recorded properties are for CuSnxInTex+2 when x = 7, with an enhanced figure of merit ZT ∼ 1.0 at 873 K.237 Alloying SnTe with different compounds can enhance its TE performance. Alloying AgI is also effective in enhancing the TE performance of SnTe. In work reported by Banik et al.,238ZT ∼ 1.05 was achieved at 856 K for 5 mol% of AgI doping with SnTe. This co-doping helps to increase the band gap, decrease the energy offset between two types of VBs, and induces effective convergence of the VBs. Tan et al.239 alloyed SnTe with AgBiTe2 by melting and SPS method. Samples of AgSnxBiTex+2 composition show a reduction in the hole concentration than in pristine SnTe when AgBiTe2 is added in a small fraction (x = 25). After a certain limit, the hole concentration starts to increase again due to the transition from solid
solution to phase segregation and the reduction in the band gap. A high ZT ∼ 1.1 was achieved at 775 K for the sample with x = 15. He et al.240 synthesized SnTe–AgSbSe2 by a zone-melting method and added 5% Cd to reduce the carrier concentration and tune the band structure of SnTe. They achieved ZT ∼ 0.92 at 820 K in SnCd0.05Te–3% AgSbSe2. Even a minimal amount of doping (∼5%) with rare earth material Yb causes effective band convergence and increases DOS near Fermi level in SnTe.241 SnTe/PbSe, SnTe/PbS, EuTe/PbTe superlattices and SnEuTe films also show better thermoelectric properties than bulk SnTe.110 Wu et al.242 reported a ZT ∼ 0.7 at 675 K in an AgPb9Sn9SbTe20 sample fabricated by MA and plasma-activated sintering (PAS) technique. Later, p-type semimetallic TAST-m system or (SnTe)m(AgSbTe2) composition materials were also found to have promising TE performance and Pb free. Han et al.243 reported ZT ∼ 1 at 710 K for m = 4 sample. Using mechanical alloying and Co-ball-milling, Falkenbach et al.244 observed promising TE performance in this class of materials. Xu et al.245 fabricated single-crystalline SnTe nanowires using the thermal vapor deposition method. They studied different nanowires with diameters ranging from 218 nm to 913 nm in a temperature range of 25 K to 300 K. Measurements of electrical conductivity and thermal conductivity of these individual systems show that electrical conductivity does not have a strong diameter dependence, but the Seebeck coefficient increases by a factor of two when the nanowire diameter decreases from 913 nm to 218 nm the Seebeck coefficient reaches 41 μV K−1 at 300 K for the thinnest nanowire. That means thermal conductivity has remarkable dependence on nanowire's diameter and is lower than regular bulk SnTe. Enhanced phonon-surface boundary scattering and phonon-defect scattering are the probable causes for this result.

5.2 SnSe-based materials

5.2.1 Crystal structure and general transport properties of SnSe. 
SnSe-based materials are applicable as optoelectronic,246,247 photovoltaic, phase change memory alloys, and in TE devices. Unlike cubic SnTe, SnSe possesses a layered orthorhombic (a ≠ b ≠ c, α = β = γ = 90°) crystal structure (space group Pnma) at room temperature.248–250 Crystal structure in this phase can be derived from a three-dimensional distortion of the rock-salt crystal structure, as shown in Fig. 17a–c.
 Fig. 17  Crystal structure of SnSe at Pnma phase (a) projection along c-axis, (b) projection along b-axis, (c) projection along a-axis (data taken from ref. 248). (d) Coordination polyhedron of Sn-in Pnma phase up to 4.1 Å. 
Each atom in this phase has the coordination environment of a heavily distorted octahedron, and the lattice is deformed NaCl type and shows anisotropic TE properties and strong phononic anharmonicity.137 SnSe is an intrinsic p-type semiconductor having an indirect band gap of ∼0.9 eV and a direct band gap of ∼1.3 eV.251,252 The lattice parameters reported for the Pnma phase are a ≈ 11.501 Å, b ≈ 4.153 Å, and c ≈ 4.445 Å.248 The band structure of SnSe is very complex. From reported DFT calculation for the Pnma phase shows that the energy difference between the first and second VB is ∼0.06 eV while between the first and third is ∼0.13 eV. From the same DFT calculation, the effective mass on different VB was estimated to be  for the first VB band and  for the second VB. So it is clear that the second VB is heavier than the first VB.253 Each cell contains two double layers perpendicular to the longest axis (here a-axis). Each layer is formed by zigzag planes of Sn–Se chains extending in two dimensions. The double-layer is held by a network of covalent bonds, whereas the interaction between two double layers is weak. The bonding between the layers is weak van der Waals type. The easy cleavage in this system is along with the (l00) planes. At the Pnma phase, each Sn atom shares six different types of interactions with its neighboring atoms (seven Se atoms and two Sn atoms and vice versa for the neighboring of each Se atom). Due to lone-pair, the structure contains highly distorted SnSe7 coordination polyhedra (containing three short and four very long Sn–Se bonds) due to lone-pair.254 Two types of interaction among those are strong. They involve short Sn–Se distances (2.7–2.8 Å) as d1, d2 (twice), two interactions involve intermediate Sn–Se distances (3.3–3.4 Å) as d3 (twice), d4, one interaction of distance (3.5 Å) as d5 (twice) between Sn–Sn contacts and an additional Sn–Se interaction of distance (4.1 Å) as d6, as shown in Fig. 17d.
As temperature increases, a continuous displacive structural phase transition occurs towards the Cmcm phase, spreading over 200 K below the critical temperature of 807 K. Around 807 K SnSe enters a five-fold coordinated Cmcm phase, as shown in Fig. 18a–c. Lattice parameters in this phase are a ≈ 4.310 Å, b ≈ 11.705 Å, c ≈ 4.318 Å, and the crystal structure has higher symmetry.250,255 From the first principle calculation, Dewandre et al.256 reported that this Cmcm-phase is only stabilized when the anharmonicity is considered, and the origin of enhancement of carrier concentration and the nature of the phase transition was also explained. Using first-principle calculation and Boltzmann transport equation, Wu et al.157 studied SnSe and found that spin–orbit coupling (SOC) significantly influences phonon anharmonicity. SOC-weakens phonon anharmonicity and reduces the delocalization of the resonant bonding network. As a result κlattice is enhanced up to ∼60%. In the Cmcm phase, the atoms form double layers similar to those in the Pnma phase. Each atom is now coordinated to four neighboring atoms at an equal distance,254 but in each layer, all Sn–Se bonds are stacked parallel, and the stacking is continued perpendicular to the b axis along with the layer (with d2 = d3 and d4 = d6), as shown in Fig. 18d. Due to this structural phase change, SnSe is applicable as phase-change memory alloys. The anisotropy of the crystal structure shows an effect on the electronic and thermal transport properties. The complex crystal structure of SnSe is the leading cause of its low lattice thermal conductivity. Shi et al.257 calculated the Pnma phase to have an indirect band gap of ∼0.829 eV and Cmcm to have a direct band gap of ∼0.464 eV, as shown in Fig. 19. The significantly smaller band gap in the Cmcm phase leads to increased bipolar conduction at high temperatures, declining its Seebeck coefficient. SnSe and similar class compounds were previously ignored in thermoelectrics due to their low electrical conductivity and ZT values. The decrease in lattice thermal conductivity with increasing temperature along all crystallographic directions suggests that lattice anharmonicity is an important phonon scattering mechanism in this compound.138,149,258
 Fig. 18  Crystal structure of SnSe at Cmcm phase (a) projection along a-axis, (b) projection along c-axis, (c) projection along b-axis (data taken from ref. 248). (d) Coordination polyhedron of Sn-in Cmcm phases. 
 Fig. 19  Quasiparticle band structures of SnSe in the (a) Pnma-phase and (b) Cmcm-phase. Pnma-SnSe has an indirect band gap of ∼0.829 eV, while the band gap of Cmcm-SnSe is direct with a magnitude of ∼0.464 eV. Reprinted from ref. 257, with the permission of AIP Publishing. 
In some research works, the origin of p-type nature in SnSe was tried to be described. According to Huang et al.,259 the DFT calculations of the formation energy and electronic properties of intrinsic defects of SnSe, including two vacancies (VSn and VSe), two interstitials (Sni and Sei), and two antisites (SnSe and SeSn) indicates that VSn can act as an effective source for p-type conduction under both Sn-rich and Se-rich conditions due to relatively low formation energy as well as a desirable ultra-shallow transition energy level.258–260 No such effective sources were found to initiate intrinsic n-type conduction. According to Duvjir et al.260 (Fig. 20), the most dominant VSn shift the Fermi energy level inside the dispersive VB and produce extra holes. On the other hand, VSe creates a nondispersive donor level and localized electrons, neighboring only two Sn atoms and a VSe site. In other words, VSe does not donate electron carriers in SnSe but generates immobile electrons localized within only a few angstroms of the vacancy site. So VSe cannot result in n-type characteristics in SnSe VSe–Sn–Se creates two fragile nondispersive flat bands, one band is occupied, and the other is empty. So VSe–Sn–Se acts similar to VSe, and overall, SnSe shows p-type properties. In 2018 Li et al.,261 with the help of first-principles calculations combined with the semi-classical Boltzmann theory, theoretically demonstrated the effect of a carrier concentration of different phases of p-type SnSe. ZT increases with the increase in carrier concentration in the range 1 × 1017 cm−3 to 1 × 1019 cm−3. A maximum ZT ∼ 1.62 at 723 K was achieved along the Pnma phase b-axis for a carrier concentration of 1.75 × 1019 cm−3 and Cmcm phase b-axis the maximum ZT ∼ 4.35 at 923 K was achieved with carrier concentration of 1.82 × 1019 cm−3.
 Fig. 20  High-resolution STM topographic images (top), top view of relaxed structure models (middle), and simulated DFT images (bottom) of (a) Sn-vacancy, (b) Se-vacancy, and (c) Se–Sn–Se vacancy. The blue and red balls represent Se- and Sn-atoms, respectively, and vacancies are indicated by open circles. Only the atoms in the top layer are displayed in all the relaxed structure models. Reprinted from ref. 260, with the permission of AIP Publishing. 
At ambient conditions, SnSe crystallizes in the orthorhombic Pnma phase. Not only temperature but also externally applied pressure affects the phase transition of SnSe. In earlier times, Chattopadhyay et al.262 reported that there would be no phase transition if the external pressure is applied on SnSe up to 34Gpa. Still, later Alptekin reported a phase transition at 7 GPa from orthorhombic Pnma to Cmcm phase.263 Agarwal et al.264 observed a pressure-dependent electrical resistance and Seebeck coefficient and found the behavioral change of these parameters at 6.5 GPa. Loa et al.265 found evidence of second-order structural phase change towards a higher symmetry space group under the high external pressure of 10.5(3)GPa in SnSe. Later, de Souza et al.266 observed nanostructured SnSe samples under a pressure variation up to 18 GPa. The study of cell parameter variation and enthalpy calculation found a phase transition near 4 GPa. After 8.2 GPa up to 18 GPa, there was no sign of phase transition. Ghosh et al.,267 in 2016, theoretically investigated the effect of high pressure on fully-relaxed samples of SnSe and got 4 GPa as the critical pressure for structural phase transition. Additionally, they found that the resistivity is highest along a-axis and lowest along b-axis, although conductivity increases with increased pressure. The semimetallic nature followed by the orthorhombic → monoclinic structural transition of SnSe above 12.6 GPa was reported by Yan et al.268 That semiconducting-semimetallic transition brought an abrupt change in carrier concentration as well as mobility of the charge carriers. Later, Xie et al.269 reported a structural phase transition ∼8 GPa and a semiconductor to semimetal electronic transition followed by a Pnma to Cmcm structural transition ∼12 GPa. According to Yang et al.,270 there is a phase transition from Pnma to Cmcm at 22 GPa. With further increase in external pressure, various novel crystal structures of SnSe were found (Pmm, C2/m, and Cmmm), only Pnma phase is semiconductor among them, and other phases are metallic. Despite so many theoretical and experimental studies, the critical pressure to initiate the phase transition is still ambiguous; this may be due to inherent microstructures or defects in the system. Monolayer (2D) SnSe also attracted tremendous attention in recent years for its promising TE properties.
Hu et al.271 designed five allotropes (honeycomb structured) of monolayer SnSe, namely α-, β-, γ-, δ- and ε-SnSe. Except ε-SnSe other allotropes are indirect band gap semiconductors. Though TE properties of α-SnSe were reported before, using the ab initio DFT method, they found all the new allotropes (β, γ, δ) have even better TE properties. The predicted ZT value for the β-SnSe phase was ∼2.06 at 300 K, which was remarkable. Later Haq et al.272 reported their study on optoelectronic and photovoltaic applications of these honeycomb allotropes. TE properties of SnSe in the mid-temperature range can be further improved by applying a slight hydrostatic pressure or uniaxial stress. In 2016 Zhang et al.273 reported their work on improving TE performance of Pnma-SnSe in all three crystal directions by applying external pressure. They showed that below 700 K applying external pressure of 6 GPa, the ZT values of p-type materials could reach ∼2.5 and ∼1.7 along b- and c-directions respectively and at the same pressure and 600 K temperature n-type conduction resulted along a direction with a ZT value of ∼1.7 at 600 K. In 2017 Rehman et al.274 reported the change in structural and optical (near/mid-infrared region) properties of cubic-SnSe due to applying external pressure up to 40 GPa. According to the report, the structural parameters decrease gradually with increased pressure in the range of 0–40 GPa. For cubic-SnSe, the band gap showed direct nature at 0 GPa, indirect nature from 5 GPa to 30 GPa, and metallic nature from 35 GPa to 40 GPa. Application of pressure can reduce the Pnma to Cmcm phase transition temperature effectively. Yu et al.275 in 2016 noticed a reduced transition temperature to ∼500 K at 4 GPa hydrostatic pressure. Band gaps of both phases vary inversely with pressure, and at 8 GPa, the SnSe band gap vanishes. This external pressure reduces the Grüneisen parameter, and the maximum reduction takes place along a-axis. Hence, κlattice increases due to the increase in hydrostatic pressure. External pressure does not affect κel remarkably as its value in Pnma-phase was inherently very small. 4 GPa pressure cannot change κtotal significantly which is advantageous to increase ZT. Uniaxial stress > 3.5 GPa along c-axis also can cause Pnma to Cmcm structural phase transition. Another work by Gusmão et al.276 reported that ZT reduces due to the external pressure. They studied SnSe-Pnma crystals below 750 K temperature under P = 0, 4, 7, 10 GPa. Improvement in TE performance of single-crystal and polycrystalline SnSe based materials due to applying different strategies is discussed in the next section.


5.2.2 TE performance of SnSe based materials. 
5.2.2.1 p-Type single-crystal SnSe. 
Single-crystal samples are advantageous than polycrystalline samples in some aspects. In single-crystal, the disturbance of grain boundary scattering and “texture” on the effects of dopants can be avoided. In a comparative study, Jin et al.277 reported a higher ZT ∼ 0.92 for single-crystal SnSe than ZT ∼ 0.5 for polycrystalline SnSe near 793 K. Agarwal et al.278 fabricated Single crystals of SnSe by a direct vapor transport and a chemical vapor transport method. They observed anisotropy in D.C. electrical resistivity for the first time in layered SnSe crystals. Shi et al.257 reported detailed band structure and various TE parameters of single-crystal SnSe. Later single-crystal anisotropic SnSe has attracted significant attention due to its record high value of ZT = 2.6 ± 0.3, 2.3 ± 0.3 and 0.8 ± 0.2 at 923 K (at Cmcm phase) measured along the b-, c- and a-axis respectively of the room-temperature Pnma-orthorhombic unit cell by Zhao et al.138 In that work, crystal parameters in the Pnma phase were taken as a = 11.49 Å, b = 4.44 Å, c = 4.135 Å, and in the Cmcm phase, those as a = 4.31 Å, b = 11.70 Å, c = 4.31 Å. Larger values of the Grüneisen parameter, i.e., strong anharmonicity in bonding, give rise to κlattice in ordered crystal structures, high carrier mobility, large Seebeck coefficient, and extremely κlattice along b- and c-axes leads to higher ZT values. The plot shows that the average Grüneisen parameter is the maximum along a-axis. In any direction κtot-decreases with increasing temperature, and at 973 K, all measured values were <0.4 Wm−1 K−1. Hole-doped SnSe is a promising thermoelectric material in the temperature range 300 K to 773 K. SnSe has non-parabolic and complex multiband valence states. Heavy p-type doping presses the Fermi level into the multiple VBs, makes them participate in the conduction process, and effectively enhances the Seebeck coefficient. The Seebeck coefficient increases from ∼+160 μV K−1 to ∼+300 μV K−1, electrical conductivity decreases from 1486 S cm−1 to 148 S cm−1 and PF also decreases from ∼40 μW cm−1 to ∼14 μW cm−1 as temperature increases from 300 K to 773 K. The highest ZT ∼ 1.34 was achieved in a device application with the hot side at 773 K and the cold side at 300 K.253 Hole doping in single-crystal of SnSe by using Na atoms as acceptors shows an increment in hole concentration up to two orders of magnitude and ZT up to ∼0.7 along the b-axis at 300 K and ZTmax ∼ 2 at 773 K.253 Enhanced thermoelectric performance was observed in the temperature range from 300 K to 800 K by doping p-type single crystals of SnSe with Na and Ag. Doping SnSe is difficult because of its layered anisotropic structure, where each SnSe layer is two atoms thin, and locally distorted bonding is present around the Sn and Se atoms. This doping lowers the Fermi level and increases the number of carrier pockets in SnSe, which collaboratively optimize the Seebeck coefficient and the electrical conductivity. The Seebeck coefficient of Na-doped crystals is comparatively smaller than the Seebeck coefficient of Ag-doped crystals. Additionally, the Seebeck coefficient of Sn0.99Na0.01Se is greater than that of Sn0.98Na0.02Se and Sn0.97Na0.03Se, which means increased doping concentration with Na leads to a reduced Seebeck coefficient in single-crystal SnSe. Still, the higher electrical conductivity of the Na-doped samples yields much larger power factors than the Ag-doped SnSe crystals. For the highest 3% Na-doped Sn0.97Na0.03Se crystal, PF reaches as high as ∼2.8 mW m−1 K−2 at room temperature and is greater than 1.38 mW m−1 K−2 in the whole working temperature range. The κlattice ∝ T−1, which is the sign of the dominance of Umklapp phonon processes. Over the temperature range from 300 K to 800 K, the ZTavg ∼ 1.17 is obtained for the Sn0.97Na0.03Se crystal with a maximum ZT ∼ 2 at 800 K.279 Later in another work, Wang et al.280 studied the result of Na and Ag doping on single-crystal SnSe prepared by a modified Bridgman method at a low-temperature regime. They found that Na is more effective in increasing the carrier concentration of SnSe. 1 at% Na-doping increases the carrier concentration up to ∼1.8 × 1019 cm−3 at 300 K and causes a ZT ∼ 0.33 at 350 K. Ag-doped SnSe single-crystal was prepared by a novel horizontal Bridgman method. Still, doping of single-crystal SnSe with Ag has a lesser effect on lattice thermal conductivity than doping with Na because Ag (z = 49) and Sn (z = 50) atoms have almost an equal size. Therefore Ag atoms have minimal effect on the phonon scattering mechanism. On the other hand, the size difference and mass difference of Na and Sn atoms significantly affect phonons' scattering. The highest ZT along a-axis was measured for Sn0.97Ag0.03Se to be ∼0.95 at 793 K, mainly due to the low κlattice near the Pnma–Cmcm phase transition temperature.281 Wei et al.282 prepared very high quality, fully dense SnSe single-crystals. DFT calculations showed an increase in band gap with an increase in temperature and metal to semiconductor transition at 600 K. Maximum ZT ∼ 1.0 at 850 K was observed along b-axis. Liu et al.283 reported the effect of Te doping on the Se-sites of single-crystal SnSe. They prepared Sn-rich Te doped SnSe single-crystals by a modified Sn self-flux method. Te-doping showed an effect on band gap as well as the carrier concentration. Though having almost the same cation/anion-ratio in Sn1.17Se and Sn1.17Se0.97Te0.027 have a significant difference in κlattice. Lowest κlattice was achieved in the Sn1.17Se0.97Te0.027 sample, and considering the result, for other samples, it was concluded that, κlattice-decreases with increasing Sn/(Se + Te) ratio.
5.2.2.2 n-Type single-crystal SnSe. 
The undoped SnSe is p-type with a carrier concentration of ∼5.3 × 1017 cm−3 at 300 K.284 Duong et al.285 fabricated p-type SnSe with carrier concentration of ∼5.2 × 1018 cm−3 at 773 K and reported that Bi-doping on Sn-sites turns it into n-type SnSe single crystals with carrier concentration that depends on Bi-content. In this case, a maximum ZT of ∼2.2 was achieved along b-axis (here, a = 11.49 Å, b = 4.44 Å, c = 4.14 Å) at 733 K with an n-type carrier concentration of 2.1 × 1019 cm−3 at 773 K. Pb dopant is also used to synthesize n-type SnSe single crystal successfully via a facile Sn-flux route. For the greater atomic radius of Pb (Z = 82), the formation of SnSe antisite defects is reduced, and hence it induces more Se vacancy (VSe). These vacancies are generated more with increasing Pb content in the samples and act as donors making the system show n-type conduction. Due to this doping, the majority of carriers are changed from hole to electron. The carrier concentration decreased from 4.2 × 1017 cm−3 in p-type SnSe to 3.3 × 1017 cm−3 in p-type Sn0.85+5Pb0.15Se and then increased to 4.1 × 1017 cm−3 in n-type Sn0.1+5Pb0.9Se. A maximum PF of ∼1.2 μW cm−1 K−1 was obtained at room temperature.286 Using DFT calculations, Tang et al.286 showed that due to the more robust metallic nature of Pb, the electrons become delocalized around Sn-atom, suggesting a much higher degree of electron delocalization in the Pb doped SnSe. Chang et al.287 reported an ultralow thermal conductivity with an outstanding PF and a maximum ZT ∼ 2.8 at 773 K in out-of-plane Br doped n-type SnSe crystal. Improvement in ZT of single-crystal (n- and p-type) SnSe-based materials is schematically represented in Fig. 21.
 Fig. 21  The highest achieved figure of merit (ZTmax) for different compositions of Single-crystal SnSe-based materials [data taken from ref. 138, 253, 277, 279–282, 285 and 287]. 
5.2.2.3 p-Type polycrystalline SnSe. 
Compared to single crystals of SnSe, lower PF is observed in polycrystalline samples of SnSe. Very low ZT ∼ 0.5 at about 823 K for undoped polycrystalline SnSe was reported by Sassi et al.258,288 Recently, hole-doped SnSe polycrystalline samples with reagents carefully purified and tin oxides removed were reported to exhibit ZT ∼ 3.1 at 783 K, which is even higher than reported ZTs for single crystal SnTe to date.289 Zhang et al.290 achieved ZT ∼ 0.47 at 703 K for polycrystalline SnSe samples synthesized by the SPS method at a sintering temperature of 823 K. A maximum ZT ∼ 1.3 and a very low κ ∼ 0.26 Wm−1 K−1 at 850 K was achieved by Tang et al.291 in a p-type polycrystalline SnSe sample fabricated by hydrothermal synthesis and SPS methods. The presence of cubic rock-salt-type precipitated phase was found inside the bulk polycrystalline SnSe sample. This new material features a microstructural hierarchy of nanoprecipitates coupled with mesoscale microstructures, which significantly reduces thermal conductivity and results in a higher value of ZT. Chen et al.292 synthesized Ag-doped p-type polycrystalline SnSe by melting and hot pressing. Doping SnSe with Ag increases its carrier concentration and reaches a maximum of 9 × 1018 cm−3 for 3% Ag doping on Sn-sites; this concentration is greater than that in undoped SnSe. Irrespective of doping, hysteresis is seen in resistivity between heating and cooling. Temperature-independent DOS effective mass was found to be 0.75 me. For the sample Ag0.01Sn0.99Se, ZT ∼ 0.6 at 750 K was achieved. Samples of composition NaxSn1−xSe with x = 0, 0.01, 0.015, 0.02, 0.03 were prepared by doping the SnSe sample by-Na by Chere et al.293via hot-pressing technique. A remarkably high room temperature carrier concentration of ∼2.67 × 1019 cm−3 was obtained for x = 0.02, and the highest PF was obtained for x = 0.015 samples. A peak ZT ∼ 0.8 was achieved at 773 K along the hot pressing direction. Co-doping of p-type polycrystalline SnSe with Na and Ag also effectively enhances the TE performance of SnSe. Wang et al.294 prepared Na0.02−xAgxSn0.98Se (x = 0, 0.005, 0.01, 0.015, and 0.02) class of samples via melting, annealing, and SPS methods. Carrier concentration was weakly temperature-dependent, and at room temperature, it reached a maximum of ∼2.66 × 1019 cm−3 for x = 0.005. The maximum PF ∼ 0.50 mW cm−1 K−2 and ZT ∼ 0.81 at 773 K was reported for Na0.005Ag0.015Sn0.98Se. PFavg ∼ 0.43 mW cm−1 K−2 and ZTavg ∼ 0.37 from 300 K to 773 K were achieved for the same sample. For the samples of composition SnSe1−xTex fabricated by melting and hot pressing, Chen et al.295 reported a decrease in band gap and an increase in lattice parameters. 6.25% Te doping can reduce the band gap from 0.643 eV to 0.608 eV for. The changing trend of the absolute Seebeck coefficient is consistent with that of the electrical conductivity of the samples as temperature increases; this is different from the behavior of the common TE materials and can be explained with the concept of phonon-drag effect as carrier concentration of the samples is less than 1017 cm−3. Te-doping is not that much efficient to improve the electrical transport properties of pristine SnSe. From this study, a maximum PF ∼ 0.7 mW cm−1 K−2 at 673 K was achieved. Wei et al.296 fabricated Na-doped SnSe1−xTex polycrystalline samples [0 ≤ x ≤ 0.2] using MA-SPS method. Doping with Na increases the carrier concentration from ∼7 × 1017 cm−3 to ∼1 × 1019 cm−3. It was found that p-type carriers have higher average energy, and they can easily get out of the traps caused by the defects at the grain boundary, which leads to higher mobility. Though Na-doping increases κ by enhancing its electronic part, it still enhances ZT greater than in pristine SnSe. A maximum ZT ∼ 0.72 for Sn0.99Na0.01Se0.84Te0.16 at 773 K was recorded. 1% K-doped SnSe polycrystalline sample was synthesized by combining mechanical alloying and SPS techniques by Chen et al.,297 and TE properties were investigated over the temperature range of 300 K to 773 K. Maximum electrical conductivity (∼3.76Scm−1), Hall mobility (∼2.55 cm2 V−1 S−1), and carrier concentration (∼9.2 × 1018 cm−3) were recorded for 1% K-doped and 8 hours of the ball-milled sample. For the same sample, an enhanced ZT ∼ 1.1 at 773 K was measured along the sample section perpendicular to the pressing direction of SPS. K-doping resulted in ZTavg ∼ 0.5 from 300 K to 773 K. Alkali-ion doping with p-Type polycrystalline SnSe shows that among Na, K, and Li-ion, the most effective is to dope with Na ion to optimize electrical properties. Hall carrier concentration due to 1% Na, K, or Li doping reaches 4.4 × 1019 cm−3, 9 × 1017 cm−3, and 1.1 × 1019 cm−3 respectively at room temperature. ZT ∼ 0.85 was achieved in 1% Na-doped, and ZT ∼ 0.80 was achieved in 1% K-doped samples at 800 K.298 Synergistic manipulation of the band structure and atomic-scale defect phonon scattering by co-doping of p-type polycrystalline SnSe was reported by Ge et al.299 to boost the TE performances of SnSe. The energy offsets of multiple VBs in SnSe were decreased when doped with Na and further decreased by co-doping with Na and K. Here, Na was selected as a dopant to enhance the electrical transport properties in addition to that K was selected as a co-dopant with Na to reduce the thermal conductivity, and the samples were synthesized via mechanical alloying and SPS. This work reported a maximum ZT ∼ 1.2 for (0.5% Na + 0.5% K) co-doped polycrystalline SnSe at 773 K. Li et al.300 co-doped polycrystalline SnSe with Na/K, Na/Li and Na/Ag differently. Maximum improved TE properties were found for Na/Ag co-doped SnSe. A carrier concentration ∼ 6.23 × 1019 cm−3 at 335 K, PF ∼ 0.73 mW m−1 K−2 and ZT ∼ 1.2 at 785 K for Sn0.98Na0.016Ag0.004Se were achieved. ZTavg ∼ 0.74 was observed in a temperature range of 335–785 K. NaCl-doping can improve the TE performance of polycrystalline SnSe. Yang et al. prepared samples of composition Sn1−xNaxSeClx by combined rapid induction melting and rapid hot-pressing technique. For 0.5 mol% NaCl-doping, the room temperature carrier concentration reached a high value of ∼3.95 × 1019 cm−3. 2 mol% NaCl-doping resulted in an improved electrical conductivity ∼ 102.5Scm−1 at 473 K. The lowest κlattice ∼ 0.27 Wm−1 K−1 at 810 K was recorded for 4 mol% NaCl doping, and the probable cause of this low value is the high phonon scattering by the nanoscale defects formed due to the presence of Cl atoms. A maximum ZT ∼ 0.84 was achieved at 810 K for 0.5 mol% NaCl-doping.301 In-is an effective dopant to improve the TE performance of various materials. In contrast to the usual results, Kim et al.302 observed a decrement in carrier concentration and increment in the band gap of SnSe due to In-doping. So PF also decreased but observed κ-values were lower than that of undoped SnSe. Shi et al.303 reported an extraordinary TE performance in polycrystalline SnSe with nanopores. The basic sample was synthesized via a facile solvothermal method. InSey nanoprecipitates were induced in the SnSe matrix. Those nanoprecipitates decomposed and generated nanopores in the SnSe matrix under high temperature, pressure, and vacuum during the SPS process. The final result was nanoporous SnSe. The low thermal conductivity value comes from the effective phonon scatterings and thermal radiation at interfaces associated with the nanopores, interfaces, and grain boundaries. High hole concentration ∼
1.34 × 1019 cm−3 was introduced due to high Sn-vacancies by self-doping. Finally, a high PF ∼ 5.06 μW cm−1 K−2, low κ ∼ 0.24 Wm−1 K−1, and ZT ∼ 1.7 at 823 K were achieved for a sample with ∼4% volume fraction of nanopores. Doping polycrystalline p-type SnSe with Ge improves its thermoelectric performance than the pristine SnSe. In a report by Wubieneh et al.,304 polycrystalline (Sn1−xGex) Se specimens were prepared by melting and spark plasma sintering (SPS) method. This doping results in an enhancement of the Seebeck coefficient and also a reduction of the thermal conductivity. A maximum ZT ∼ 0.77 was obtained at 800 K for (Sn0.99Ge0.01)Se. Introduction of Grain Boundaries (GB) significantly affects thermoelectric performance because of the scattering effect on electron and phonon transport in 2D and 3D materials. GB can induce different defect trapping gap states inside the band gap, which increases the potential energy barriers of carriers and reduces the degeneracy of CB valley, and hence the Seebeck coefficient gets reduced. Sun et al.128 doped polycrystalline SnSe with Si/Ge. Doping with Ge or Si can significantly shift the trapping gap states due to the strong orbital coupling between two Ge or Si atoms, thus enhancing the electrical conductivity. In addition, band convergence in the conducting bands and phonon scattering by Ge/Si doping can synergistically enhance the power factor. In another work reported recently by Zhao et al.,305 the disadvantages of polycrystalline SnSe have been reduced via synergistic Ag doping and Ge alloying. The carrier concentration of polycrystalline SnSe was enhanced to a maximum of ∼1 × 1019 cm−3 in Sn0.99Ag0.01Se at 793 K by Ag doping (at first Sn1−xAgxSe samples with x = 0–0.03 were prepared). Then the Seebeck coefficient was increased by Ge-alloying (Sn0.99−yAg0.01GeySe, y = 0–0.02). These resulted in the highest PF ∼ 10 μW cm−1 K−2 and ZT ∼ 1.5 at 793 K for Sn0.975Ag0.01Ge0.015Se sample. For polycrystalline SnSe doped with Zn, enhancement of ZT was observed by Li et al.306 For samples with composition ZnxSn1−xSe, ZT was reported to reach ∼0.96, 0.84 and 0.78 for x = 0.01, 0.02 and 0.03 respectively at 873 K, ZTmax ∼ 0.96 at 873 K for Zn0.01Sn0.99Se. There are many reported works on Cu-doping with polycrystalline SnSe. ZT ∼ 0.7 ± 0.02 at 773 K and κ ∼ 0.27 Wm−1 K−1 was achieved by Singh et al.307 for cu-doped SnSe samples prepared by fusion and SPS method. The second phase of Cu2Se formed in the SnSe matrix was the predicted reason for improving TE properties. Gao et al.308 prepared p-type Sn1−xCuxSe (0 ≤ x ≤ 0.03) polycrystals via melting and high pressure (6.0 GPa) sintering (HPS) method. Maximum PF ∼ 378 μW m−1 K−2 and ZT ∼ 0.79 was achieved at 823 K for Sn0.97Cu0.03Se sample. An ultralow κlattice ∼ 0.2 Wm−1 K−1 and ZT ∼ 1.2 at 873 K were achieved in the Cu0.01Sn0.99Se sample by Gong et al.309 Polycrystalline Sn1−xCuxSe samples were prepared using ball milling and hot pressing by Li et al.310 Unlike doping with Ag, doping of polycrystalline SnSe with Cu decreases the carrier concentration of SnSe at room temperature. Still, it enhances the carrier concentration above 523 K, which signifies that Cu acts as an effective p-type dopant at high temperatures. A maximum ZT of ∼0.66 at 813 K for Sn0.98Cu0.02Se perpendicular to the pressing direction was reported. In another recent work, Shi et al.311 reported the solubility of Cu to be 11.8% in single-crystal SnSe microbelts synthesized by a facile solvothermal method. Among the samples of composition Sn1−xCuxSe (0 ≤ x ≤ 0.118), a high ZT value of ∼1.41 was achieved at 823 K for Sn0.882Cu0.118Se. Other than Na, K, Cl, Ag, Te, Ge, Zn, and Cu, some other dopants have also been proven effective for SnSe to improve its TE properties. One such dopant is Pb. Tang et al.312 prepared phase-separated Sn1−xPbxSe materials by hydrothermal synthesis. A maximum ZT ∼ 1.7 and ∼1.4 at 873 K in polycrystalline SnSe + 1% PbSe was achieved along the direction parallel and perpendicular to the pressing direction, respectively. Polycrystalline SnSe alloyed with PbSe and then doped with Na was proved to show promising thermoelectric performances. SnSe–PbSe alloys of composition Sn1−xPbxSe were prepared by Wei et al.313 Samples remained as a single-phased solid solution up to 12 at% PbSe alloying. A low κlattice ∼ 0.85 Wm−1 K−1 at 300 K was achieved for x = 0.12. Above this concentration of PbSe κ- increased as a second phase arose and the band gap reduced. Further, polycrystalline (Sn1−xPbx)0.99Na0.01Se samples were fabricated via melting, annealing, and pulverizing followed by SPS. A maximum ZT ∼ 0.85 at 800 K was obtained in the x = 0 sample. Lee et al.314 observed a reduction in transition temperature from Pnma to the Cmcm phase when polycrystalline SnSe was doped by PbSe. In the Sn1−xPbxSe (0 ≤ x ≤ 0.2) samples, the transition temperature reduces from 777 K to 716 K when x changes from 0 to 0.2. A maximum ZT ∼ 1.2 at 773 K and ZTavg ∼ 0.5 from 300 K to 773 K was achieved in Na0.01(Sn0.95Pb0.05)0.99Se. Tl-doping is an effective approach to increase the hole concentration in SnSe. Using hot pressing, Kucek et al.315 synthesized polycrystalline samples with composition Sn1−xTlxSe (for x = 0 to 0.04). Thermal and electrical properties were studied over the temperature range from 300 K to 725 K. The doped material reaches ZT ∼ 0.6 at 725 K for x = 0.015. Gao et al.316 reported an enhancement of ZT of polycrystalline SnSe by Sm-doping. Thermoelectric transport properties of Sn1−xSmxSe (for, x = 0, 0.01, 0.02, 0.03) samples synthesized by melting and high pressure (6.0 GPa) sintering (HPS) method were investigated. ZTmax ∼ 0.55 was reported for Sn0.97Sm0.03Se reaches at 823 K. Introduction of Cd in p-type polycrystalline SnSe induces Sn vacancies which act as p-type dopants. Cd atoms reduce the formation energy of Sn vacancies and hence lower down the Fermi level into the VBs, generating holes. Compositions of Cd-doped SnSe can then be described as Sn1−xCdxSe (where x = 0, 0.005, 0.01, 0.015, 0.02, 0.025, 0.03, 0.035). ZT of ∼1.7 at 823 K was achieved by Shi et al.317 for a sample with nominal composition Sn0.97Cd0.03Se.
5.2.2.4 n-Type polycrystalline SnSe. 
In n-type SnSe, due to its 3D charge and 2D phonon transports,287,318 a higher out-of-plane ZT value can be achieved. Enhancement of TE properties in n-type polycrystalline SnSe alloys by optimizing carrier concentration and designing textured microstructures using MA and repeated SPS method was reported by Shang et al.318 The lowest κlattice ∼ 0.36 Wm−1 K−1 was obtained at 783 K perpendicular to texture direction. Enhancement of PF to 681.3 μW m−1 K−2 was observed in perpendicular and parallel directions to the texture in Sn1.005Se0.94Br0.06 at 783 K, this leads to a maximum ZT ∼ 1.5 at 783 K. In a reported work, Ge et al.319 demonstrated how three-step doping turned polycrystalline p-type SnSe into n-type, and then the TE performance was enhanced. Sn1−xRexSe0.93Cl0.02 (0.005 ≤ x ≤ 0.030) bulk samples were fabricated using a solid–state reaction and SPS method. A maximum ZT ∼ 1.5 was achieved in Sn0.97Re0.03Se0.93Cl0.02 at 798 K, and it was highest among all n-type polycrystalline SnSe. Br doped n-type polycrystalline of the composition SnSe1−xBrx (x = 0, 0.06, 0.08, 0.1, and 0.12) shows better TE performance due to synergistic effect of carrier concentration optimization and phonon scattering. A maximum ZT ∼ 1.3 at 773 K was achieved in SnSe0.9Br0.1 along the hot pressing direction.320 Br has nearly equal ionic radii as of Se [Se2− (1.98 Å) and Br− (1.96 Å)]. Chang et al.321 found that the PF can be enhanced from ∼2 μW cm−1 K−2 for undoped SnSe to ∼5 μW cm−1 K−2 for 3% Br doped SnSe. Further alloying with Pb enhances the PF up to ∼ 7 μW cm−1 K−2. The peak ZT ∼ 1.2 was achieved at ∼773 K for SnSe alloyed with 10% Pb. Doping the polycrystalline SnSe by I has also proved very beneficial for achieving better TE performance from SnSe. I-doped n-type SnSe polycrystalline samples with SnSe1−xIx as nominal composition were synthesized by melting and hot pressing (where, x = 0.01, 0.02, 0.03, and 0.04). Here I act as a donor dopant at 773 K electron concentration reaches ∼2.4 × 1017 cm−3 in SnSe0.096I0.04. Thermal conductivity became more anisotropic than the Seebeck coefficient in SnSe0.097I0.03, and a ZT ∼ 0.8 was achieved along the hot pressing direction. Further alloying with 10% S and doping with 3% I (SnSe0.87S0.1I0.03) can increase ZT ∼ 1 at 773 K.322 Doping with some specific compound has also been proved to enhance the TE performance of SnSe; one such compound, BiCl3, was reported by Wang et al.323 Thermoelectric properties of SnSe0.95–x% BiCl3 (for x = 0, 0.1, 0.2, 0.4, 0.6) samples were studied and a ZT ∼ 0.7 at 793 K in the SnSe0.95–x% BiCl3 system where x = 0.4 was achieved. Li et al.324 prepared Sn1−xPrxSe polycrystals by ball milling and hot pressing. Initially, a ZT ∼ 0.7 at 773 K was achieved for x = 0.03. After hot forging the sample, a ZT ∼ 0.9 at 773 K along the hot pressing direction was achieved. Shen et al.284 reported MoCl5 as an effective dopant to enhance the TE performance of n-type polycrystalline SnSe. They prepared SnSe0.95-x% MoCl5 samples via melting and hot-pressing methods. For x = 1.5, the n-type carrier concentration was raised to ∼1.76 × 1019 cm−3, and a ZT ∼ 0.66 at 773 K was achieved for x = 1. In the case of the SnSe polycrystalline samples synthesized by the arc-melting method, a sign change in temperature-dependent Seebeck coefficient comes near 580 K, which indicates the transformation of p-type SnSe into an n-type SnSe. A high ZT ∼ 1.8 at 816 K was achieved in n-type SnSe.325Fig. 22 and 23 Schematically represent the TE performance of various polycrystalline SnSe based materials.
 Fig. 22  The highest achieved figure of merit (ZTmax) for different compositions of p-type polycrystalline SnSe-based materials [data taken from ref. 258, 289–294 and 296–317]. 
 Fig. 23  The highest achieved figure of merit (ZTmax) for different compositions of n-type polycrystalline SnSe-based materials [data taken from ref. 284 and 318–325]. 

5.3 SnS-based materials

5.3.1 Crystal structure and general transport properties of SnS. 
SnS has the same layered orthorhombic crystal structure as SnSe.250,326,327 At lower temperature, SnS belongs to a Pnma phase with crystal parameters a = 4.334 Å, b = 11.200 Å, c = 3.987 Å,328 and passes through a structural phase change to Cmcm phase at 878 K.329 The indirect band gap is 1.09 eV for the Pnma phase and 0.42 eV for the Cmcm phase.329,330 The band structure of SnS in the Pnma and Cmcm phases are shown in Fig. 24. There is also an isotropic, metastable π-cubic phase of SnS with lattice parameter a = 11.7 Å. The cubic phase of SnS-is isotropic and has a direct band gap of ∼1.3–1.7 eV.331–333 SnS shows p-type conduction properties with hole concentration of (∼1017 to 1018 cm−3).334,335 Like SnSe, there is an existence of external pressure-induced phase transition in SnS. Ehm et al.336 examined SnS using angular dispersive synchrotron powder diffraction up to a pressure of 38.5 GPa and reported an orthorhombic to monoclinic structural phase transition at 18.15 GPa. Tan et al.337 found the ZT value for pure SnS to be ∼0.16 at 823 K. Application of different strategies improved TE performance of SnS. Comparative TE performance of various SnS based material is schematically represented in Fig. 25.
 Fig. 24  Band structures of SnS in the (a) Pnma-phase and (b) Cmcm-phase. The zero energy is set to the valence-band maximum. The highest VB and the lowest CB are highlighted in red. In the figures above, G, Z, T, Y, S, X, U, R describe the (0 0 0), (0 0 1/2), (−1/2 0 1/2), (−1/2 0 0), (−1/2 1/2 0), (0 1/2 0), (0 1/2 1/2), (−1/2 1/2 1/2) high-symmetry points respectively. Reproduced from ref. 329 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry. 
 Fig. 25  The highest achieved figure of merit (ZTmax) for different compositions of SnS-based materials [data taken from Table 3]. 


5.3.2 TE performance of SnS based materials. 
The TE properties of SnS are improved when doped with Ag. Ag-doped SnS of the composition SnS–x% Ag (x = 0, 0.05, 0.25, 0.5, 1, and 2) samples were synthesized by mechanical alloying and SPS method. ZT ∼ 0.6 at 923 K was achieved for SnS–0.5% Ag.338 In another recently reported work on Ag doping with SnS, very low κ values have been reported. κ ∼ 0.58, 0.46 and 0.34 Wm−1 K−1 were achieved in undoped, Sn0.995Ag0.005S and Sn0.99Ag0.005S respectively. ZT ∼ 1.1 at 877 K for Sn0.99Ag0.005S sample was obtained.339 There are many ways to improve a material's TE performance, such as carrier concentration optimization, band structure engineering, second phase coupling, in situ nanostructure, solid solution, etc. In the case of SnSe, the last method is proved to be significantly effective. In solid solutions, defects coming from atomic disorders act as scattering centers for phonons, which causes the reduction in κlattice. SnS1−xSex (0 < x< 1) solid solutions were fabricated via melting method. These solutions possess an orthorhombic structure and show phase transition from the Pnma to the Cmcm structure at high temperatures, similar to its constituent compounds. Lattice constants of the solid solutions increase with the increase in Se content. Value of ZT increases from ∼0.19 to ∼0.82 parallel to the pressing direction and from ∼0.16 to ∼0.62 perpendicular to the pressing direction in SnS and SnS0.2Se0.8 sample, respectively, at 823 K.340 Zhou et al.341 showed that Na-doping on Sn-sites could enhance the carrier concentration of SnS up to ∼1019 cm−3. For the Sn1−xNaxS (0 ≤ x ≤ 0.03) class of samples, peak ZT ∼ 0.65 was achieved at 850 K. Na-doping on the Sn-site increased hole concentration from ∼8 × 1017 cm−3 in pristine SnS to ∼2 × 1019 cm−3 in Na0.02Sn0.98S. In another work, Tang et al.342 synthesized polycrystalline Na-doped SnS was with enhanced TE performance using the improved chemical co-precipitation method. Different filtration conditions were explored to adjust the content of doped Na cation in SnS bulks. As a result, Maximum PF ∼362 μW m−1 K−2 and ZT ∼ 0.52 at 873 K were recorded for the centrifuged and triple-washed sample. Systematic Se-alloying and Na-doping with SnS resulted in ZT ∼ 1.1 at 820 K in Na0.02Sn0.98S0.5Se0.5. Se-alloying introduced weak bonds and large mass leading to low sound velocity and the disappearance of frequency gap and the large Grüneisen parameters. On the other hand, Na doping optimized carrier concentration.145 Using DFT calculations, Xiao et al. found that doping of p-type SnS by aliovalent dopants like Sb and Bi are not effective in transforming its conduction in n-type, but isovalent Pb-substitution of the Sn sites can change the type of majority carriers and make the conduction in SnS n-type.343 Later experimentally, it was shown that isovalent Pb substitution on Sn-sites of polycrystalline SnS thin films on glass substrates gives rise to n-type conduction with carrier concentration ∼ 1012 cm−3 to ∼1015 cm−3.344 Hu et al.345 fabricated n-type polycrystalline SnS bulk materials using MA and SPS methods and studied their TE properties. A maximum PF ∼ 1.2 μWcm−1 K−2 and a maximum ZT of ∼0.17 at 823 K were obtained for the polycrystalline SnS0.98Br0.02. Bi-doping increases the PF and κel of SnS, at the same time, it decreases κlattice. As an overall effect, ZT of SnS is enhanced due to Bi-doping. In a theoretical by Sun et al.,329 a maximum ZT ∼ 1.63 and ∼1.59 at 1080 K was achieved in p-type and n-type SnS, respectively. While at low temperature, ZTavg ∼ 0.31 was recorded. Recently Guan et al.346 have reported a maximum ZT ∼ 0.36 at 800 K in Sn1−xBixS sample with x = 1.56%. Wang et al.347 proposed a new route to improve the TE performance of SnS. They used the chemical precipitation method to synthesize SnS and used Na2S2O3 and SnCl2 as raw materials. The lowest thermal conductivity ∼0.29 Wm−1 K−1 and highest ZT ∼0.41 were achieved at 848 K. The new polymorphs of single-layered SnS have drawn attention in recent studies as they have excellent TE properties. In 2019 Haq et al.348 fabricated five energetically equal stable monolayered polymorphs as α-, β-, γ-, δ- and ε-SnS. These polymorphs (excluding β-SnS) show giant in-plane anisotropy in different thermoelectric parameters and are predicted to be used as promising TE materials in the future.
A high concentration of Sn-vacancies herms the TE performance of SnTe. Optimizing the Sn-vacancy concentration is essential to improve its TE performance. TE performance of SnTe in low dimension with suitable dopants are to be studied in detail. More research on n-type SnTe is yet to be conducted to use SnTe for device application. Highly anharmonic and layered-orthorhombic SnSe encourage searching for new compounds with similar features to be a candidate material for good TE performance. The TE performance of SnSe nanosheet and thin films on different substrates should be thoroughly examined. Successful application of SnSe as a filler material for enhancing the power factor of polymers opens new directions in the research of organic–inorganic hybrid TE materials with this class of materials. SnS can be a good TE for commercial application due to its low cost, low toxicity, and abundance, but low electrical conductivity hinders its TE performance. Various novel strategies to improve electrical transport properties can be applied to poly and single crystals of SnS. More investigation on these materials' mechanical and chemical stability is yet to be done for their successful large-scale device application.

6. Ge chalcogenides as TE materials
Germanium chalcogenides (GeTe, GeSe, and GeS) are important representative materials for TE application in the mid-temperature region. This section has discussed the progress of Ge-chalcogenide-based materials for TE application in past years.
6.1 GeTe-based materials

6.1.1 Crystal structure and general transport properties of GeTe. 
GeTe is one of the most efficient TE materials for mid-temperature (600–923 K) and near-room temperature applications.349 GeTe has a rocksalt crystal structure (Fmm, paraelectric, centrosymmetric) when T > 720 K with lattice constant a ≈ 6.01 Å. Near 720 K without any change in the number of atoms per unit cell GeTe undergoes a phase transition from a high-symmetry cubic-phase (c-GeTe or β-GeTe) at higher temperature to a low-symmetry rhombohedral-phase (a = b = c, α = β = γ < 90°, R3m, ferroelectric, noncentrosymmetric, r-GeTe or α-GeTe) at lower temperature through slight distortion along the [111] direction,350,351 accompanied by an angular distortion of the unit cell from 60° to about 57.51° as shown in Fig. 26.
 Fig. 26  Schematic crystal structure for the cubic-to-rhombohedral phase transition in GeTe. Reproduced with permission from ref. 378. Copyright © 2014, American Chemical Society. 
This r-GeTe has lattice parameter a = 5.985(2) Å and rhombohedral angle α = 88.17(3)° near 300 K.350 These phase transitions are an ideal example of displacive ferroelectric phase transition due to the relative shift of the cation and the anion sublattices caused by the softening116,352 of the transverse optic (TO) phonon mode at the zone-center.350,353 Usually, high-symmetry materials have the advantage of high band degeneracy which causes a good improvement in PF. On the other hand, low-symmetry materials usually show low κlattice. In a reported work, Li et al.354 proposed that the positive contributions of both (r- and c) phases for enhancing ZT can be enjoyed simultaneously by slight symmetry breaking. During phase transition in GeTe, the symmetry breaking is minimal, so it causes both the advantages to be utilized in the same material simultaneously. The band structure of direct band gap c-GeTe is very similar to SnTe with two VBs (L and Σ), and this two VB configuration is also applicable for r-GeTe but switch in energy levels between the L and Σ bands. If ΔEL–Σ is the energy offset between two VBs, then ΔEL–Σ = −0.174 eV for r-GeTe and ΔEL–Σ = +0.064 for c-GeTe. For L-band of c-GeTe, the band degeneracy found to be NVL = 4 similarly for Σ-band it is NVΣ = 12. Due to the symmetry reduction in r-GeTe, those 4L pockets split into 3L + 1Z, and those 12Σ pockets split into 6Σ + 6η, so it seems to be difficult to achieve effective band convergence, but the added new degrees of freedom is enough to achieve effective band convergence. This makes r-GeTe as good thermoelectric material as c-GeTe or even better.355
Jeong et al.356 identified four possible crystal structures of GeTe using DFT calculation during its phase transition. Among these, R3m and Fmm were already known, but the other two phases P1 and Cm, were identified for the first time. From band structure calculation, an equal band gap (∼0.80 eV) was reported for both R3m and P1. While Cm was reported to have a lower band gap of ∼0.66 eV and Fmm to have a band gap of ∼0.34 eV. Band structures of all possible phases are shown in Fig. 27. Askarpour et al.357 used the first-principles modelling and reported unusual thermoelectric transport anisotropy in rhombohedral GeTe. According to their report Seebeck coefficient is lesser anisotropic than electrical conductivity and PF. They took parallel and perpendicular to [111]-direction as cross-plane and in-plane directions, respectively. They found that in n-type GeTe, the cross-plane electrical conductivity is larger than that for in-plane, and for p-type, the nature is opposite. This anisotropy causes ZT to differ depending on the direction. But pristine p-type degenerate GeTe is not considered a good thermoelectric material. It has an extremely high carrier concentration (∼1021 cm−3) due to intrinsic Ge-vacancies caused by the large population of the occupied anti-bonding states.358 The high carrier concentration of GeTe reduces the Seebeck coefficient and hence PF. From the phase-diagram analysis of GeTe, Miller,359 Lubell and Mazelsky360 proposed the presence of only one type of defect. According to them, each defect acts as an acceptor and supplies two holes to the VB. Later Damon et al.361 proposed the defects to be Ge-vacancies. In contrast to that, analyzing Hall measurements, Kolomoets et al.362 reported the presence of more than one type of defect. Later Lewis et al.363 analyzed transport properties of GeTe as a function of composition based on a two-carrier model. In recent work, Liu et al.364 clarified the role of Ge-vacancies in structural change and TE performance. Using first-principles calculations and Boltzmann transport theory, they found that Ge-vacancies are more likely to form spontaneously in the cubic phase than in the rhombohedral phase, plays a vital role in stabilizing the cubic phase, and Ge-vacancies modify the band structure in a way that reduces power factor.364 From reported data, we can have an idea about the different TE parameters of GeTe. Pristine GeTe exhibits the σ value of ∼8067 S cm−1 at 300 K, which decreases to ∼2158 S cm−1 at 708 K. S-value of ∼34 μV K−1 at 300 K increases with temperature, then reaches ∼153 μV K−1 at 708 K. GeTe has κlattice ∼ 2.6 Wm−1 K−1 and κtotal ∼ 8.3 Wm−1 K−1 at 300 K, which reduces to ∼3.43 Wm−1 K−1 at 673 K. ZT of pristine GeTe increases with temperature from ∼0.03 at 300 K to ∼0.8 at 720 K.365 The size-dependence of the amorphous to crystalline phase transitions in GeTe nanoparticles increases the possibility of designing memory devices with GeTe at the nanoscale. Yarema et al. reported that crystallization temperature increases with decreasing size of the GeTe nanoparticles below 10 nm.366 Recently, Shekhawat et al.367 have reported that Se-substitution in GeTe, increased the band gap in the as deposited films and decreased in the annealed films. These results have proved that Se-substitution can effectively be used to tune the bandgap and transition temperatures of GeTe systems. It will be interesting to explore the transport properties of these GeTe1−xSex (0 ≤ x ≤ 1.0) alloys both by experimentally as well as theoretical DFT method.
 Fig. 27  Calculated band structure of each crystalline structure for (a) R3m, (b) P1, (c) Cm, and (d) Fmm-GeTe. Reproduced from ref. 356. 


6.1.2 TE performance of GeTe based materials. 
6.1.2.1 Results of using Bi and Sb as doping or a co-doping agent. 
The introduction of Bi-was proved to enhance the TE performance of pristine GeTe. In the case of Ge1−xBixTe (0 ≤ x ≤ 0.10) samples, the replacement of Bi3+ ions in Ge2+ sublattice helps to reduce excessive hole concentration and tune the Seebeck coefficient towards better TE performance. Low κlattice ∼ 1.1 Wm−1 K−1 at 300 K was achieved for x = 0.10, and ZT ∼ 1.3 at 725 K was obtained for x = 0.06 by Perumal et al.174 For Ge1−xBixTe name samples with (0 ≤ x ≤ 0.12), Wei et al.349 reported ZT ∼ 1.91 at 740 K for x = 0.1. Bi-dopants reduce the concentration of holes by substituting Ge sites and also tune Fermi level to a more suitable position to enhance PF. By manipulating Ge-vacancies and Ge-precipitates, Jin et al.368 successfully improved the TE performance of GeTe. They optimized the carrier concentration ∼ 2.79 × 1020 cm−3 by Bi-doping, then Ge-phase aggregation was suppressed by proper heat treatment. As a result, maximum ZT ∼ 1.9 at 723 K was eventually obtained in Ge0.94Bi0.06Te. Recently Zn/Bi co-doping has also been proved to enhance TE performance of pristine GeTe synergistically. A peak ZT∼ 2.0 at 700 K and ZTavg ∼1.35 between 400 K and 800 K were achieved in the Ge0.9Bi0.06Zn0.04Te sample. Bi-optimizes the carrier concentration and also reduces c/a ratio, which causes band convergence. Increased point defect scattering and emerged inter-valley scattering of the converged VB maxima due to Bi/Zn co-doping reduces κlattice ∼ 0.44 Wm−1 K−1 at 650 K.369 Co-doping of GeTe with In/Bi can lead to an enhanced ZT ∼ 2.1 at 723 K and ZTavg ∼ 1.3 in from 300 K to 723 K in the composition Ge0.93In0.01Bi0.06Te. In-dopants enhances S (∼120 μV K−1 at 300 K) by forming resonant levels. On the other hand, Bi dopants reduce κlattice (∼0.5 Wm−1 K−1 at 300 K) by forming point defects that cause more phonon scattering.370 TE performance of GeTe was also improved when alloyed with CdTe. Here, CdTe helps to gain effective band convergence in both (r- and c-) phases, further Bi doping in Ge-sites tunes carrier concentration. Substitution of Cd and Bi in Ge-sites increases phonon scattering and leads towards a reduced value of κlattice (∼0.6 Wm−1 K−1). As a result, ZTavg ∼ 1.2 from 300 K to 800 K was achieved.371 In a reported work, Hong et al.127 analytically demonstrated the role of planar vacancies in scattering mid-frequency phonons and experimentally showed that pristine GeTe doped with Cd/Bi could cross the amorphous limit of lattice thermal conductivity. The combined effect of high-density planar vacancies, grain boundaries, and point defects leads to a high ZT ∼ 2.2 in (Ge1−x−yCdxBiyTe) composition samples. Xing et al. achieved ZT ∼ 2.5 at 700 K (Fig. 28) and ZTavg ∼ 1.5 in from 300 K to 750 K for Ge0.9Mg0.04Bi0.06Te. A large mass and strain field fluctuations introduced by Mg/Bi co-doping resulted in an ultralow thermal conductivity. Co-doping with Mg/Bi optimized the carrier concentration and improved the DOS effective mass.372
 Fig. 28  The highest achieved figure of merit (ZTmax) for different compositions of GeTe-based materials [data taken from Table 4]. 
Another way of decreasing the phase transition temperature of GeTe below 300 K is co-doping it by Mn/Bi. Liu et al.373 achieved ZT ∼ 1.5 at 773 K and ZTavg ∼1.1 from 300 K to 773 K in cubic Ge0.81Mn0.15Bi0.04Te. The synergistic effect of Ti doping on the TE performance of GeTe was demonstrated by Shuai et al.374 Maximum ZT ∼ 1.2 was achieved in Ge0.98Ti0.02Te, and when Ge0.99Ti0.01Te was further doping with Bi high, ZT ∼1.6 was achieved for Ge0.93Ti0.01Bi0.06Te at 623 K. A solid solution between PbTe and GeTe was studied by Woolley et al.375 It was found that GeTe-PbTe alloys exhibit good thermoelectric performance. Pb reduces Ge vacancies, and thermal conductivity is reduced. On the other hand, Bi2Te3 works as a good TE material near room temperature. Properties of (GeTe)1−x(Bi2Te3) solid solutions with 0 ≤ x ≤ 0.05 were studied by Christakudi et al.376 in a temperature range of 80 K to 350 K. Bi2Te3 based alloys are suitable TE materials in the near room temperature region. Bi2Te3-GeTe pseudo-binary compounds effectively shift the operating temperature range to a higher temperature region. Kohri et al.377 observed that the temperature at which ZT of Bi2Te3 was reaching maximum shifts to a higher value in Bi2Te3-GeTe pseudo-binary compounds. Later, a high ZT ∼ 1.9 at 773 K for Ge0.87Pb0.13Te + 3 mol% Bi2Te3 was achieved by Wu et al.378ZT ∼ 1.1 was achieved at 773 K in Ge0.87Pb0.13Te. Further doping the alloy with Bi2Te3 enhanced its TE performance. Introduction of Bi2Te3 increases the solubility of PbTe in the GeTe matrix enhances point defect scattering hence reduces κlattice and also reduces the energy difference between two VBs.379 Gelbstein et al.176 reported a maximum ZT ∼ 1.2 at 723 K in Ge0.5Sn0.25Pb0.25Te sample. They further reported that ZT can reach 2.1 for ∼5% Bi2Te3 doped-Ge0.87Pb0.13Te sample.379 In another work, Wu et al.125 successfully fabricated a complex hierarchical microdomain-nanodomain-gap structure in GeTe–Bi2Te3 pseudo-binary alloy after proper heat treatment. Before and after heat treatment, they examined Ge vacancies, gaps, and ferroelectric domain structures that originate due to the complex interplay between strain and electrostatic interactions during the phase transition of the sample.
