A peak ZT ∼ 2.4 at 773 K and ZTavg ∼ 1.28 from 323 K to 773 K was achieved for GeTe alloyed with 5% Bi2Te3 doped GeTe or (Ge0.95Bi0.05Te1.025), which was greater than that of (ZT ∼ 2.0) same sample fabricated without heat treatment. Zhou et al.124 theoretically showed that GeTe/Bi2Te3 superlattice material shows low κlattice and has an impact of being used as good TE material. A systematic study by Li et al.380 on the synergetic effect of Pb doping and thermal annealing with GeTe–Bi2Te3 showed that Pb effectively reduces carrier concentration and enhances PF, and also reduces κlattice by introducing point defects. A maximum ZT ∼ 1.47 was reached at 673 K for the (Ge0.84Pb0.16Te)0.93(Bi2Te3)0.07 pseudo-cubic phase alloy before annealing, for the same sample at the same temperature ZT reduced to 1.35 at 673 K. The annealing process led to a reduction in density of stacking faults (plane defect), which consequently increased κlattice. It was previously said that GeTe and its alloys work as good thermoelectric material in the mid-temperature range but quenched p-type (Ge0.87Pb0.13Te)1−x(Bi2Te3)x with [0 ≤ x ≤ 0.07] samples shows good TE performance near room temperature as well mid-temperature region. Samples were fabricated by the usual alloying, quenching, and SPS process. ZT ∼0.96 and 1.38 at 300 K and 373 K respectively and ZTavg ∼1.15 from 300 K to 473 K for x = 0.07 was achieved in (Ge0.87Pb0.13Te)0.03(Bi2Te3)0.07 sample.381 Inherently GeTe shows p-type conduction, but alloying of AgBiSe2 with GeTe can result in n-type conduction. For p-type and n-type (GeTe)100−x(AgBiSe2)x systems ZT ∼ 1.3 at 467 K and ZT ∼ 0.6 at 500 K respectively were reported by Samanta et al.382 Liu et al.364 also reported n-type conduction in GeTe when heavily doped with Bi. ZT ∼ 2.4 at 600 K (Fig. 28) and ZTavg ∼1.5 over a temperature region from 300 K to 800 K were achieved in Ge0.86Pb0.1Bi0.04Te by Li et al.354via controlling the distortion degree to make the split low-symmetry VBs in r-GeTe converge by Pb alloying and Bi doping. Doping with Bi-reduced carrier concentration, increased the rhombohedral angle, made the band structure more favorable for electronic performance, and increased phonon scattering by introducing point defects.354 Improved TE performance of (GeTe)1−x(Bi2Se0.2Te2.8)x systems where GeTe was doped with Bi2Se0.2Te2.8 was investigated by Koenig et al.383 Doping with another IIA element, Sc increases the PF up to a value of 23.3 μW cm−1 K−2 at 300 K in Ge0.94Sc0.06Te by reducing carrier concentration without affecting rhombohedral distortion degree. Though Bi dopants reduce that PF to a value of 16.8 μW cm−1 K−2 in Ge0.88Sc0.06Bi0.06Te, they also effectively reduce κlattice due to strong point defect scattering. As a net result, ZT is enhanced. Liu et al. first theoretically explained the role of rhombohedral distortion degree on the Ge-vacancy formation energy. They found from DFT calculations that a Ge-vacancy has the lowest formation energy among all other typical defects, which decreases more with an increase in rhombohedral angle and can become negative at a certain point. Sc-dopants do not change the formation energy significantly. They experimentally showed the effect of Sc/Bi co-doping on GeTe, and a ZTavg ∼1.2 from 300 K to 723 K was achieved.384
Another Group-V element Sb is also an effective dopant for GeTe. Perumal et al. reported the effects of doping GeTe with Bi, Sb individually or co-doping with both. Doping of GeTe with either Bi- or Sb-showed a similar trend of powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD) patterns. No second phases were detected in both cases, and PXRD was indexed into R3m phase at room temperature. From the measured lattice parameters for the similar composition Ge0.95Bi0.05Te and Ge0.95Sb0.05Te, it has been reported that Bi-elongated the a- and b-axes faster than shortened the c-axis, while Sb-did it oppositely. From DSC measurements of the samples described that Bi-doping promotes R3m → Fmm transition of GeTe slightly faster than Sb doping. Rhombohedral to cubic phase transition temperatures for Ge0.95Bi0.05Te and Ge0.90Sb0.10Te were 656 K and 634 K; respectively, both are lesser than pristine GeTe. Finally, a ZTmax of ∼1.8 at 725 K was achieved for the co-doped sample Ge0.85Bi0.05Sb0.10Te.385 Substitution of Sb3+ ions at Ge2+ site in GeTe decreases the p-type carrier concentration.
Reported work on high-quality crystalline ingots of p-type Ge1−xSbxTe showed various effects of doping with Sb. As a dopant, Sb-reduced the carrier concentration, increased the cubic nature at room temperature, lowered the rhombohedral to the cubic phase transition temperature, enhanced the band degeneracy. κlattice was also reduced due to increased phonon scattering from various nanoscale/mesoscale structures formed due to Sb-doping. All these effects led to a ZT ∼ 1.85 at 725 K when x = 0.10.386 A peak ZT ∼ 2.2 at 740 K in Sb doped GeTe has been reported by Vankayala et al.387 for Ge0.92Sb0.08Te single crystals. The extreme reduction in κlatticewas explained theoretically as well as experimentally in this work. Sb-doping softens the phonon frequency, changes the dominant scattering mechanism from a four-phonon to a three-phonon and κlattice-reaches its amorphous limit at high temperature. TE properties of GeTe-based materials can be enhanced by tuning the phase transition temperature via doping with Sb.388 Sb has the advantage to effectively optimize the carrier concentration, as already discussed. Sb-can promote the crystal symmetry of GeTe to enlarge the band degeneracy while flattening the valence band. As a consequence, effective mass increased to 2.34 m0 (at room temperature in Ge0.875Sb0.125Te). Point defects and mass fluctuations, introduced by doping with Sb, also decreased the κlattice by suppressing the phonon propagation. Finally, the peak ZT ∼ 1.8 at 773 K and a ZTavg ∼1.1 from 300 K to 773 K were achieved in the Ge0.9Sb0.1Te sample.388 When pristine GeTe is doped with Sn-the rhombohedral to cubic structural phase transition temperature is reduced to 557 K. Total thermal conductivity reduces significantly for Ge0.9Sn0.1Te near room temperature.389 For amorphous GeTe, increasing Sn content decreased the band gap, and a PF ∼ 2.789 × 103 μW m−1 K−2 was achieved at 300 K. For the crystalline film of GeTe, the PF ∼ 1.423 × 103 μW m−1 K−2 at 718 K was achieved, as Sn-doping increases effective mass in this case.390 Sharply peaked DOS of low-dimensional materials are very effective in modifying their electronic transport properties. But it is challenging to achieve such DOS in 3D-bulk materials as they have DOS as a smooth function of energy (DOS ∝ E1/2). Interestingly, the Rashba spin-split systems possess features of lower-dimensional structures in their DOS. The Rashba effect occurs in inversion-asymmetric materials that have strong spin–orbit coupling. Recently Hong et al.391 have demonstrated how the “Rashba effect” in Sn doped GeTe samples can enhance their TE performance. Atomic site displacement in the samples leads to the introduction of the Rashba effect in this case. As a result, band convergence and hence remarkable increment in PF was obtained, and further doping with Sb-optimizes the carrier concentration. Low κlattice Was resulted due to the association of grain boundaries, nano precipitates, and stacking faults. As a net result, a ZT ∼ 2.2 in Ge0.87Sn0.05Sb0.08Te was achieved at 740 K. GeTe was doped with Sb and alloyed with GeSe to tune the carrier concentration over a large scale of 1 × 1020 to 3 × 1021 cm−3. Li et al.392 fabricated polycrystalline samples like Ge1+δTe (−0.04 ≤ δ < 0.06), Ge1−xSbxTe (x ≤ 0.12), and Ge1−xSbxTe1−ySey (x ≤ 0.15, y ≤ 0.20) and achieved a maximum ZT ∼ 1.7 at 725 K, which was predicted to be further increased up to ∼2.0 by additional Se alloying. ZT ∼ 2.2 in Ge1−x−ySbxZnyTe by Bi doping and Zn alloying with pristine GeTe was reported by Hong et al. Sb reduces the band gap between acoustic and optical phonon branches, which plays a vital role in decreasing κlattice and Zn doping enhances band convergence and enhances PF.156 Alloying MnTe into GeTe, a class of Ge1−xMnxTe (0 ≤ x ≤ 0.21) compounds was prepared by melting–quenching-annealing-SPS method, and with the increasing proportion of MnTe, a shift from r-phase towards c-phase was observed near room temperature. MnTe-increased the band convergence, effective mass, Ge-vacancy, and alloy scattering, on the other hand, reduced the τ of phonon and phonon velocity. Further doping with Sb-optimized the carrier concentration and lowered κlattice. As an overall effect, a maximum ZT value of 1.61 at 823 K was achieved for Ge0.86Mn0.10Sb0.04Te.393 According to a report by Rosenthal et al.179 Ge12(Sb1−xInx)2Te15 (x = 0, 0.5, 0.75, 1.0) solid solution series also shows improved TE performance. Li et al.394 showed that Sb-is an effective doping agent to optimize carrier concentration. Sb-dopant manipulates the degree of rhombohedral lattice distortion to maximize the band degeneracy and increases the solubility of PbTe in the GeTe matrix. In this case κlattice was reduced due to scattering of phonons by Ge/Pb substitutional defects, and ZT > 2 at T < 800 K was achieved in both r- and c-phases for GeTe alloyed with PbTe and then doped with Sb. Ga-doped and Ga/Sb co-doped GeTe samples have promising TE performance. Using hybrid flash-SPS method on the sintered co-doped samples of Ge1−xGaxTe (x = 0.00–0.07) and Ge1−x−yGaxSbyTe (x = 0.02; y = 0.08, 0.10) materials were synthesized by Srinivasan et al.395 Room temperature Hall-measurement of these samples showed that effective mass increases from 1.30 me to 2.38 me for changing (x = 0, y = 0) to (x = 0.02, y = 0.08). A maximum ZT ∼ 1.95 at 723 K and ZTavg ∼ 1.85 in the temperature range from 600–773 K was achieved in the Ge0.90Ga0.02Sb0.08Te sample, which can be explained as an increase in band degeneracy and hence PF.395 Xing et al.396 co-doped GeTe with Mg/Sb, and a maximum ZT ∼ 1.84 at 800 K and ZTavg ∼ 1.2 in 300–800 K was achieved in Ge0.85Mg0.05Sb0.1Te. In addition to this, a TE uni-leg was fabricated by one-step sintering of the Ge0.85Mg0.05Sb0.1Te powder, Ti powder, and Ni powder directly using the hot-pressing technique and a thermal cycling test was performed, which showed quite a high service stability even after 450 thermal cycles. Nshimyimana et al.397 reported that partial substitution of Ge by Cd and further doping with Sb is an effective way to enhance the TE performance of GeTe. In this case, Cd-increases the effective mass of holes by reducing offset energy between two types of VBs and increasing their contribution in the conduction process in cubic and rhombohedral phases. Cd and Sb both enhance phonon scattering by introducing point defects which lead to a low κlattice. In addition to this, Sb optimizes the carrier concentration also. With all these effects ZT ∼ 1.8 at 700 K and ZTavg ∼ 1.3 from 400 K to 800 K were achieved for the Ge0.89Cd0.03Sb0.08Te sample. Doping with Re-improved mechanical properties of GeTe for practical device applications. Co-doping with 10% Sb and 2% Re resulted in a maximum ZT ∼ 1.7 at 773 K.398 The rhombohedral to cubic phase transition temperature of GeTe also can be tailored via Sn-alloying leading to significantly improved performance of thermoelectric properties of GeTe. Further doping the cubic Ge0.5Sn0.5Te matrix with Bi and Sb resulted the suitable combination of favourable electronic properties as well as low thermal conductivity and enabled a peak ZT ∼ 1.7 at 723 K and ZTavg ∼ 1.23 from 400 K to 800 K for Ge0.4Sn0.4Bi0.02Sb0.12Te.399
In 2006 Matsunaga et al.400 showed that GeTe(1 − x)-Sb2Te3(x) pseudo-binary compound films maintain rocksalt crystal structure over a wide range of compositions for a range 0 ≤ x ≤ 2/3 when subjected to instantaneous heating by laser irradiation followed by equally rapid cooling. Many works reported that GeTe rich Sb2Te3 pseudo-binary [Sb2Te3(GeTe)n]-type materials show excellent TE properties in the mid-temperature region. Temperature-dependent phase transition of these classes of materials with n = 6, 12, 15 were studied by Schneider et al.401 Rosenthal et al.402 described this structure as a mixture of tetradymite-type Sb2Te3 and GeTe, and this results in the pseudo-binary homologous series Sb2Te3(GeTe)n with distorted rocksalt-type unit-cell of a thickness depending on the richness (n-value) of GeTe in the sample, and each cell is separated from the adjacent cell by the van der Waals gap. The Te–Te layers are separated by van der Waals gaps for every n block of the Ge–Te layer in the Ge–Sb–Te structure; this is absent in the traditional Sb doped GeTe samples. Samples with n = 2 and 19 were found to achieve ZT ∼ 1.3 at high temperatures. But, the lack of long-time stability of those samples above room temperature, these materials were not so effectively used in actual TE devices. On the other hand, ZT reached only up to ∼0.7 in that temperature region where the samples were long time stable and applicable in devices. Fahrnbauer et al.403 demonstrated the effect of CoGe2 precipitates on the TE properties of (Ge–Sb–Te) samples. A simultaneous increase in PF and decrease in κlattice due to homogeneously distributed CoGe2-precipitates resulted in ZT ∼ 0.9 at 523 K and ∼1.8 at 723 K in the case of (CoGe2)0.22Sb2Te3(GeTe)19 heterostructure. At the same time, peak ZT ∼ 1.9 at 723 K was recorded for (CoGe2)0.20Sb2Te3(GeTe)17. Vacancy engineering on Sb2Te3(GeTe)17 leads to a high ZT. In their work, Xu et al.404 varied the annealing time of the sample and found ZT ∼ 2.4 at 773 K and ZTavg ∼ 1.51 for 7 days' annealing. The carrier concentration of the same sample reduces after annealing. The microstructural origin of this carrier concentration reduction lies in the migration and reconstruction of intrinsic Ge vacancies present in the system during the annealing process, which in turn forms more stable defects named long-range van der Waals gaps. Reduced carrier concentration reduces κel. Recently, Rhenium (Re) doped Sb2Te3(GeTe)12 has been reported as good TE material of Ge–Sb–Te class by Lou et al.405 with a ZT ∼ 2.25 at 773 K in Sb2Te3(Ge0.988Re0.012Te)12. Tsai et al.406 reported that TE performance of Ge1−xSbxTe and (GeTe)1−y(Sb2Te3)y samples can be drastically enhanced by using a two-step cooling procedure. For example, Ge0.95Sb0.05Te alloy synthesized by this procedure with an intentional suspension in cooling for an hour at 673 K and resulted in a ZT ∼ 2.0 at 720 K, which is greater than that achieved in another work mentioned in this report previously. When these samples were further doped with Sb2Te3, their TE performance was enhanced. For (GeTe)0.95(Sb2Te3)0.05ZT reached ∼2.5–2.9 at 720 K. The reason behind this enhancement is the composition fluctuation of alloys below a specific temperature, which is a sign of spinodal decomposition. Sb/In co-doping reduces the phase-transition temperature to extend the better thermoelectric behavior of cubic GeTe to low temperature. ZT ∼ 2.3 was achieved above 650 K in Ge0.89Sb0.1In0.01Te.126 Qiu et al. reduced the phase transition temperature of GeTe from 660 K to 523 K via entropy engineering. Multiple doping by In, Pb, Sb, and I increased the configurational entropy of GeTe. ZT ∼ 2.1 at 800 K was achieved for the highest entropy species Ge0.84In0.01Pb0.1Sb0.05Te0.997I0.003. The increased configurational entropy improved TE performance in many ways. It helped to form short-range disordered microstructures and provided all-scale phonon scattering centers that consequently reduced the κlattice. The large effective mass of the high entropy sample resulted in a high Seebeck coefficient.407
6.1.2.2 Research progress on TAGS materials. 
Since the past few decades, TAGS [generally expressed as (AgSbTe2)100−x(GeTe)x or TAGS-x] materials have been proving themselves as good TE materials to use mainly in the mid-temperature regime. TAGS-80 and TAGS-85 were considered the two most promising systems of this class.178,408–410 Undoped TAGs-75 has a ZT ∼ 0.7 at 625 K. Zybała177 reported that ZT value increases to a maximum of ∼1.2 at 625 K when TAGs-75 is optimally doped with AgSbSe2 with a composition (GeTe)75(AgSbTe2)23.75(AgSbSe2)1.25. Christakudis et al.411 achieved a maximum ZT ∼ 1.68 at 700 K in (GeTe)0.8 [(Ag2Te)0.4(Sb2Te3)0.6]0.2. TAG-85 is rhombohedral at room temperature and transforms to cubic after a certain higher temperature. Kumar et al.408 prepared cubic TAG-85 even at room temperature by following a specific cooling procedure during synthesis. They reported that TAGS-85 works best when spontaneous precipitation of Ag8GeTe6 is minimized. In recent years researchers found interest in improving further TE performance of this class of samples using various strategies. Levin et al.409 reported that doping with rare earth Dy improves TE performance of TAGS-85. For undoped TAGS-85, a ZT ≤ 1.3 was recorded at 730 K. At the same temperature, the TAGS-85 sample doped with 1% Dy for Te showed ZT ≤ 1.5, and TAGS-85 doped with 1% or 2% Dy for Ge showed ZT ≥ 1.5. TAFS-85 becomes a dilute magnetic semiconductor when doped with 1 at% Ce or Yb. Though this doping increases κlattice but the comparatively higher increment in S compensates for the drawback, and as the net effect, a high ZT ≈ 1.5 at 700 K was achieved in TAGS-85 + 1% Ce and TAGS-85 + 1% Yb.410 When the Ag/Sb ratio is changed from unity, TE performance is improved than the conventional TAGS. Doping TAGS-75 with AgSbSe2 improves the TE performance due to a reduction in κlattice and increment in Seebeck coefficient.178
6.1.2.3 Other effective dopants and techniques. 
In another work by this research group 1.5 at% BiI3-doped Sb2Te3(GeTe)17 has been reported to have a good ZT value of ∼2.2 at 723 K and ZTavg ∼1.4 in the temperature range of 323–773 K. Doping with BiI3 induces anion site (Te) vacancy clusters which combine with cation site (Ge) vacancies resulting in significant phonon scattering and hence a reduction in κlattice.412 Among IIIA elements (Al, Ga, In, Tl), doping with In-has been proved to be most efficient in lowering the carrier concentration, enhancing the Seebeck coefficient, reducing κel to result in an enhanced ZT in GeTe. Solid solutions of composition (In2Te3)x(GeTe)3−3x were prepared by Sun et al.413 and proposed as an effective substitute for TAGs as a p-leg material for TEGs or TECs. ZT ∼ 1.35 at 823 K for x = 0.05 was achieved. The energy difference between the light VB and heavy VB decreases with increasing In2Te3 content. Increasing DOS causes enhancement in the Seebeck coefficient but a reduction in electrical conductivity. In the rhombohedral phase, In-doped GeTe shows very similar resonant levels as those in undoped GeTe. An enhanced total DOS in both phases originates from the host–dopants interaction irrespective of crystal structure or temperature. The deep defect states (DDSs) above the VBM are around the Fermi levels, and the DOS changes cause an enhanced Seebeck coefficient. A maximum ZT ∼ 1.3 at a temperature about 628 K in Ge1−xInxTe class of samples when x = 0.02 was achieved.414 Co-doping With In/Se is also effective to synergistically enhance the TE performance of GeTe. For Ge0.99In0.01Te1−ySey (y = 0.05 and 0.10) class of samples ZT ∼ 1.38 was achieved for y = 0.05. In dopants incorporates resonant levels and increases PF while Se decreases it. But as Se decreases κ- also, the net result is enhanced ZT.415 From the calculated carrier concentration of different metal-tellurides, it is clear that the carrier concentration increases with a decrease in cation size. That's why among PbTe, SnTe and GeTe, the last one has the highest carrier concentration, which is a drawback for GeTe. Li et al.416 proposed that effective size enhancement of cation can improve TE performance of GeTe. Ge-vacancy has the lowest formation energy and the biggest cause of its high carrier concentration. Doping with PbSe increases cation size, reduces anion size, uplifts the formation energy of Ge-vacancies, and helps to reduce high carrier concentration. Finally, ZT ∼ 2.2 has been achieved in GeTe–PbSe alloys. Ge1−xAlxTe (0 ≤ x ≤ 0.08) alloys were processed via melting and SPS by Srinivasan et al.417 The outcome of the studies suggests that Al-is not a good dopant for GeTe like other IIIA elements. Al-dopants increase the hole concentration, increase energy separation between L and Σ band, and reduce the band convergence, resulting in a reduced Seebeck coefficient and ZT. Additionally, they found IIA element Ba-is also not a good dopant to increase TE performance of GeTe. In a recent work, Gao et al.418 doped GeTe with transition element Y and achieved a ZT ∼ 1.2, further enhanced by doping with Bi and Sb to ZT ∼ 1.8 and ZTavg ∼ 1.1 from 300 K to 723 K. When GeTe is doped with SnSe and SnS, the system acts as a solid solution at a lower concentration of SnSe and SnS, and at their higher concentration, the coexistence of solid solution and phase separation is observed. Further doping with Sb-optimizes carrier concentration, increases the solubility of SnS– SnSe in GeTe. An abundance of point defect is also increased, and micron-sized Ge-rich precipitates are introduced. So κlattice is further reduced due to an increased phonon scattering in Sb-doped (GeTe)1−2x(SnSe)x(SnS)x system. At 300 K κlattice ∼ 0.8 Wm−1 K−1 and ∼0.3 Wm−1 K−1 were achieved for (GeTe)0.95(SnSe)0.025(SnS)0.025 and (Ge0.90Sb0.10Te)0.95(SnSe)0.025(SnS)0.025 systems respectively. ZT ∼ 1.9 at 710 K was achieved for the second system.419 Recently an enhancement in PF has been repor ted by charged ferroelectric domain walls (DWs) in GeTe.420

6.2 GeSe-based materials

6.2.1 Crystal structure and general transport properties of GeSe. 
GeSe is an inherently p-type semiconductor with a layered orthorhombic crystal structure when crystallized under ambient conditions.421 At low temperature, it remains in the Pnma phase with lattice parameters a ≈ 10.825 Å, b ≈ 3.833 Å, c ≈4.388 Å.250,422 GeSe is an indirect band gap semiconductor with an experimentally measured band gap of 1.14 eV.423 GeSe has complex VB structures. According to the reported DFT calculation, the energy difference between the first–second and the first–third VB are ∼0.13 eV and 0.16 eV, respectively. From the same DFT calculation, the effective mass on different VB for orthorhombic GeSe was estimated to be  for the first VB band and  for the second VB. Similar to SnSe, in this case, also the second VB is heavier than the first VB.424 Gashimzade et al.425 reported a phase transition in GeSe from simple orthorhombic to body-centered orthorhombic (Pnma → Cmcm) at 29 GPa. Under some specific crystallization condition, GeSe can also be crystallized in two other phases, and those are cubic (Fmm) and rhombohedral (R3m), respectively. Sist et al.426 carried out a complete structural study of GeSe from 120 K up to 940 K using high-resolution synchrotron powder X-ray diffraction and found a structural phase transition to a rocksalt crystal structure (Fmm) at 907 K. According to Wiedemeier and Siemers, the first-order phase transition from orthorhombic-GeSe to cubic-GeSe occurs at 924 ± 5 K under ambient pressure. It remains in the cubic phase till the temperature reaches its melting point.427,428 This high-temperature cubic phase was inherently unstable due to imaginary vibrational modes centered at the BZ center, and the cubic phase was not available under ambient temperature and pressure. An increment in external pressure leads those imaginary vibrational modes to disappear, and the phase transition occurs under ambient temperature.429 Bhatia et al.430 reported a pressure-induced first-order transition from layered crystalline semiconductor-GeSe (α-phase) to a metallic-GeSe (Fmm) at 6 GPa with a ≈ 6.42 Å. But analyzing in situ x-ray-diffraction measurements, Onodera et al.431 observed that GeSe remains in the orthorhombic structure up to 82 GPa. Von Rohr et al.432 synthesized and characterized a new, unusual β-phase of GeSe, a polymorph of α-GeSe. This β-phase also has a layered orthorhombic crystal structure. The intralayer Ge–Se distance is ∼2.59 Å, and that for interlayer is ≈3.27 Å, where each layer consists of six-rings with the vertices arranged in a “boat” conformation, and it is a semiconductor with a moderate band gap. Later Xu et al.433 worked on this new β-phase of GeSe and investigated the electronic, optical, and transport properties. In 2018 Yu et al.434 discovered two novel intermediate phases between the phases reported by Von Rohr et al. (α-phase → R3m → Fmm → β-phase). Where the low-pressure R3m phase shows ferroelectricity and high pressure (6 GPa) Fmm phase acts as a 3D topological insulator. In recent times by analyzing the phonon dispersion curves, Kagdada et al.435 reported that the Pnma → Fmm phase transition takes place at 7.3 GPa. There is a conflict regarding the actual value of the external pressure, though, from the reported data, it can be predicted to lie somewhere above 6 GPa. A minimum κlattice ∼ 0.39 Wm−1 K−1 was speculated for GeSe,421 which was lower than that of GeS (0.52 Wm−1 K−1) and SnS (0.45 Wm−1 K−1). In practice, the low carrier concentration is a barrier for pristine GeSe to achieve promising TE performance.


6.2.2 TE performance of GeSe based materials. 
Hao et al.424 predicted that ZT for hole-doped GeSe along crystallographic b-axis can be enhanced up to 0.8 at 300 K and 2.5 at 800 K (Fig. 29) if the carrier concentration can be increased from 4 × 1019 cm−3 to 6.5 × 1019 cm−3. Those dopants which can increase the carrier concentration of GeSe are suitable to enhance the TE performance of GeSe. Ag doping with GeSe on the Ge sites is an effective way to improve its TE performance. Zhang et al.436 showed that doping with Ag can enhance the carrier concentration ∼1018 cm−3 (which still does not meet the requirement). Still, other dopants like Na, Cu, La, Se cannot significantly increase the carrier concentration, even like Ag. For Ge1−xAgxSe systems, the Hall carrier concentration increases with increasing x and reaches a saturation value at x = 0.002. Further doping with Sn increases carrier concentration a little more. ZT ∼ 0.2 at 700 K was achieved in Ge0.79Ag0.01Sn0.2Se. According to some reports, Na can also act as an effective dopant for GeSe to reduce thermal conductivity. Polycrystalline samples of composition Ge1−xNaxSe with x = 0.00, 0.01, 0.02, and 0.04 synthesized by Shaabani et al.437 reported that the value of κ decreases with increasing Na content and becomes lowest ∼ 1.06 Wm−1 K−1 at 300 K and lowest κlattice ∼ 0.44 Wm−1 K−1 at 573 K among the synthesized samples at x = 0.04. Previously it was predicted that the cubic phase of GeSe could not be stable under ambient conditions. Still, later Roychowdhury et al.438 showed that alloying pristine GeSe with specific compound AgBiSe2 could stabilize its cubic phase even under ambient conditions. For (GeSe)1−x(AgBiSe2)x class of samples [0.35 ≤ x ≤ 0.50] this stable cubic phase exists. Additionally, this alloyed material showed n-type conduction, a counterpart of inherent p-type GeSe, and was urgently needed to use GeSe in commercial TEG or TEC devices. As a result of this alloying, with the increase in the AgBiSe2 content, band gap gradually decreases from 1.1 eV (orthorhombic phase) to 0.05 eV (for 0.08 ≤ x ≤ 0.29, rhombohedral phase) and then increases to 0.3–0.4 eV (for 0.30 ≤ x ≤ 0.50, cubic phase). A maximum ZT ∼ 0.45 at 677 K was achieved for the n-type cubic sample for x = 0.50. In 2019 Cui et al.439 theoretically predicted a decent power factor in the cross-plane direction for n-type GeSe. More delocalized interlayer carrier density was assumed to cause the highly normalized electrical conductivity and hence PF. Additionally, they found group VA and VIIA elements to be effective n-type dopants for GeSe. However, VIIA elements were more effective than VA elements to normalize the PF towards a higher ZT. Different phases of GeSe also have properties that are helpful to use as better TE material than pristine GeSe. A way to improve the TE performance of orthorhombic GeSe is to alloy with some particular material to stabilize a new rhombohedral structure (like GeTe) that will have multi valley Fermi surface increased carrier concentration. Two such promising alloying agents are AgSbSe2 and AgSbTe2. Huang et al.440 found a transition from orthorhombic phase to rhombohedral phase in pristine GeSe when doped with AgSbSe2. This alloying leads to an abrupt increase of ∼1020 cm−3 in carrier concentration of GeSe. ZT ∼ 0.86 at 710 K was achieved in the GeAg0.2Sb0.2Se1.4 system. Also, alloying with AgSbTe2 can synergistically improve the TE performance of GeSe. A maximum ZT ∼ 0.96 at 754 K was achieved in GeSeAg0.2Sb0.2Te0.4, and the ZTavg ∼ 0.64 was recorded between 301 K and 754 K by Yan et al.441 Synthesis of p-type crystalline cubic GeSe was done by Yan et al.442 by alloying with Sb2Te3. Study of (GeSe)1−x(Sb2Te3)x alloys at room temperature. The study revealed how different phases of SnSe could be fabricated by changing the percentage of Sb2Te3 in the alloys. When x = 0%, the sample remains in the Pnma phase. At x = 5%, there is a coexistence of Pnma and cubic phases. When x > 8%, the sample turns out to be in the complete cubic phase. From this study, ZT ∼ 0.73 was achieved in (GeSe)0.92(Sb2Te3)0.08 at 710 K. Under ambient conditions, Sarkar et al.443 stabilized the rhombohedral phase of GeSe by allying with 10 mol% AgBiSe2. A remarkably high ZT ∼ 1.25 at 723 K was achieved parallel to the growth direction in the p-type rhombohedral (GeSe)0.9(AgBiSe2)0.1 crystals synthesized by the Bridgman method. Theoretically, it was predicted that this alloy has some ferroelectric instability, which softens the polar transverse optical phonon modes and scatters more heat-carrying acoustic phonons. Which results in a reduction of κlattice, and leads to show a better TE performance. In another work by Sarkar et al.444R3m-phase of GeSe was stabilized under ambient conditions by alloying GeSe with AgBiTe2. This phase change comes along with a change in the nature of bonding from covalence to metavalence. Rhombohedral (GeSe)0.9(AgBiTe2)0.1 has some metallic character and closeness to ferroelectric instability. Hence, its band gap, electronic structure, and low κlattice are preferred to be used as a good TE material. A large PF of 12.8 μW cm−1 K−2 and ZT ∼ 1.35 was achieved in (GeSe)0.9(AgBiTe2)0.1 at 627 K. Wang et al.445 studied the structure-dependent TE properties in GeSe1−xTex (0 ≤ x ≤ 0.5) samples. Orthorhombic, hexagonal, and rhombohedral phases were obtained for different Te content in the compounds. Pure orthorhombic phase exists for x ≤ 0.05, for x = 0.10 and 0.15 both orthorhombic and hexagonal phases coexist, pure hexagonal Ge4Se3Te phase for 0.20 ≤ x ≤ 0.35 and rhombohedral phase for x ≥ 0.40. Different structures had different carrier concentrations, e.g., for the Ge-rich orthorhombic phase, the carrier concentration is ∼1016 cm−3, and for the other two Ge-poor phases, those are ∼1021 cm−3. Albeit having an equal range of carrier concentration rhombohedral phase has a higher Seebeck coefficient than the hexagonal phase due to the favored band structure. At 778 K, a maximum ZT ∼ 0.69 was achieved in GeSe0.55Te0.45 after transitioning from the rhombohedral phase to the cubic phase. Using the first-principles-based Boltzmann transport equation approach, Yuan et al.446 predicted ZT values of p-type orthorhombic GeSe along a-, b- and c-direction can be enhanced up to 1.54, 1.09, and 1.01 at 700 K and 8 GPa, respectively. Predicted values are remarkably higher than those at zero pressure at an experimental carrier concentration of ∼1018 cm−3. Yuan et al.447 again investigated electronic and phononic transport properties along with the dynamical and mechanical stability of GeSe in its R3m phase. Along the interlayer direction at 800 K, the predicted maximum ZT values for p-type and n-type doping were 2.02 and 2.37, respectively. According to Li et al.,448 strain-engineering is an effective way to improve the TE performance of 2D monolayer GeSe. The effects of different kinds of strain like uniaxial, biaxial, tensile, compressive, armchair-compressive strain are demonstrated in this work. It was predicted that the PF could be enhanced four times at room temperature for both p-type and n-type 2D monolayer GeSe at the −6% strained condition. Reduction in structural dimension can improve TE performance of GeSe. A recent investigation on 3D-GeSe and 2D polymorphs of GeSe (α-GeSe, β-GeSe, and γ-GeSe) showed that both 3D and 2D structures are equally stable and can break the benchmark limit of ZT ≈ 1 at room temperature. At room temperature, ZT ∼ 1.02 for 3D- GeSe and ZT ∼ 1.10 for γ-GeSe were achieved. ZT for γ-GeSe reached ∼1.72 at 150 K.111 Crystal structures and band structures of all such polymorphs are shown in Fig. 30.
 Fig. 29  The highest achieved figure of merit (ZTmax) for different compositions of GeSe-based materials [data taken from Table 5]. 
 Fig. 30  The top and side view of the optimized crystal structure of (a) α-GeSe, (b) β-GeSe and, (c) γ-GeSe. Electronic band structure of (d) 3D-GeSe, (e) α-GeSe, (f) β-GeSe and, (g) γ-GeSe. Reprinted from ref. 111. Copyright (2019), with permission from Elsevier. 

6.3 GeS-based materials
GeS has the same layered orthorhombic crystal structure as SnSe, SnS, or GeSe with lattice parameters a = 10.481 Å, b = 3.646 Å, c = 4.299 Å for low-temperature Pnma phase.250,449 There is a structural phase transition from the Pnma phase to the Cmcm phase at 863 K.423 GeS has an experimentally calculated band gap of ∼1.65 eV.450 The energy offset between the first and second VB is ∼ 0.11 eV. The estimated effective masses are  for the first VB band and  for the second VB.424 The first VB is heavier than the second one, which was not in the case of GeSe or SnSe. So GeS shows some conduction properties that are different from GeSe and SnSe. Using a constant-pressure ab initio technique, the gradual phase transition towards the Cmcm-phase of the layered Pnma-GeS was found by applying external hydrostatic pressure about 35 GPa.451 The work also predicted the possibility of further transition into a rocksalt-structured phase from Pnma and Cmcm by sliding the layers relative to each other along c-direction. In 2019 Haq et al.450 reported the presence of two new thermodynamically stable polymorphs (γ-GeS and ε-GeS) of monolayer GeS along with the normal α-GeS. These two new polymorphs have extremely high DOS near the Fermi level and exhibit a high Seebeck coefficient and sufficiently high electrical conductivity. The plots of DOS and different TE parameters of those polymorphs of GeS concerning chemical potential are depicted in Fig. 31. Using DFT and Boltzmann transport theory, the ZT reaches 1.67 for γ-GeS at 500 K and 1.07 for ε-GeS at 1000 K. Ding et al.421 and Shafique et al.453 also studied the thermoelectric and phonon transport properties of GeS along with some other promising IV–VI metal chalcogenides.
 Fig. 31  Plot of (a) total DOS, (b) thermopower or Seebeck coefficient, (c) electrical conductivity, (d) thermal conductivity of the three types of GeS monolayers (α, γ, and ε) determined from Boltzmann transport theory against chemical potential. Reproduced from ref. 452. Copyright © 2019, American Chemical Society. 
Ge-chalcogenides are promising materials for TE application. The high intrinsic p-type carrier concentration of GeTe is a drawback of this material. Research to find suitable dopant and doping techniques are encouraging for this material to optimize carrier concentration and achieve an efficient n-type counterpart for device application. GeTe is the only candidate in IV–VI class of chalcogenides that exhibit a rhombohedral (ferroelectric, noncentrosymmetric) phase at ambient conditions. The transition from ferroelectric to paraelectric (cubic) phase has great importance in the thermal transport properties of this material. Critical analysis of the thermal behavior of this material in the transition region is required. The large band gap and strong covalent bonding in orthorhombic GeSe harm its TE performance. Change in phases (rhombohedral, cubic) tailors the nature of bonding and improves TE properties of GeSe. More research can be pursued to tune and stabilize the suitable phase of GeSe for TE applications. GeSe and GeS have a similar crystal structure as SnSe; their promises in organic–inorganic hybrid TE materials are to be investigated. Techniques to improve the mechanical stability of these materials for real-life applications are to be researched more.
The current discussion focuses on the experimental and theoretical development of the thermoelectric properties of Ge chalcogenides. These materials in the thermoelectric module for power generation technologies may face additional commercialization challenges due to germanium, an expensive material. The device efficiency and commercial feasibility of these materials are yet to be thoroughly investigated.
7. Perspective and future aspects
In this review, we have briefly discussed various novel and traditional strategies that are being used nowadays to improve TE performance beyond the classical benchmark by optimizing the electrical and thermal transport properties of suitable materials. Various strategies, as, increase in local DOS and distortions in DOS by the resonant level formation, relaxation time modulation for charge carriers, lowering of deformation potential, lowering of band effective mass and increment of band degeneracy, modulation of ‘Diffusion of carriers’ and ‘phonon-drag’, reduction of dimensionality of the materials, carrier energy filtering, charge density wave formation to improve the power factor of materials and reduction of specific heat of the material, reducing phonon relaxation time by increment in scattering rate of phonons or large anharmonicity, reduction in phonon group velocity, nanostructure engineering, all-scale hierarchical architectures, reduction of bipolar thermal conduction, tuning of atomic disorders to reduce thermal conductivity of a material have been discussed here for general TE materials. We also have discussed how these strategies have been validated on eco-friendly Pb-free IV–VI metal chalcogenide (SnTe, SnSe, SnS, GeTe, GeSe, and GeS) materials to improve their TE performance. Despite colossal research progress for some candidate materials (SnTe, SnSe, GeTe) of this class, still, some routes for further improvements in the TE performance of these materials in the future can be proposed.
(i) Strongly coupled TE parameters in 3D-bulk TE materials are problematic to decouple. Meanwhile, TE parameters of 2D and quasi-2D materials can be decoupled more easily. So, a reduction in the dimensionality of the materials can help to improve the TE performance. Already single-layered or bi-layered (2D) SnSe, SnS, GeSe, GeS have shown promising TE performance as their 3D-bulk counterpart and sometimes possess equally stable crystal structures in both dimensions. This technique can be explored on other metal chalcogenide materials to check their efficiency in lower dimensions.
(ii) It is essential to fabricate p-type and n-type counterparts of a material to use in TE devices. Both counterparts for some materials (SnSe, GeSe) of this class have already been researched widely, and the rest are yet to be explored more.
(iii) From our discussion, we have seen that a change in fabrication technique can significantly change TE performance in the same composition material. Changing the synthesis method can lead to a better TE performance. More research on the synthesis techniques can be pursued to improve the purity of the desired samples to achieve better TE performance from materials.
(iv) Environmental concerns due to the toxicity of inorganic materials are increasing the research interest in organic TE materials. Using various conducting polymers, carbon nanotubes (CNTs), graphene, etc., as a matrix for the degenerate semiconductor nanoparticles, a new direction has already been evolved to find efficient TE materials for eco-friendly commercial use. Bi2Te3, Bi2Se3, etc., are efficient inorganic chalcogenide compounds used in organic–inorganic composite materials for TE applications. Recently, SnSe NS/PEDOT:PSS composites have been reported to have promising TE performance. Some other IV–VI metal chalcogenides have similar layered lattice structures like SnSe. The TE performance of organic–inorganic nanocomposites with those materials is yet to be explored.
(v) Best operating condition and best structural stability may not fall in the same temperature region [e.g., SnTe, Sb2Te3(GeTe)n]. The lack of long-time stability of such samples in the best operating temperature regime resists applying those materials in actual TE devices. It is to be inspected how the temperature region of stability and best operation can be merged and if organic TE materials can be used for this purpose.
(vi) Phase transition significantly impacts the transport property of materials. The phase with high symmetry usually has the advantage of high band degeneracy and high density of state effective mass, which helps to achieve an elevated Seebeck coefficient. The low symmetry phase usually has the advantage of low lattice thermal conductivity.354 Phase transition of a TE material in the operating temperature range can be harmful to practical applications. The immediate change in the thermal expansion coefficient during phase transition will generate high internal stress between the devices' materials and outside contacts. It would decline the performance of the device. GeTe is a promising material for mid-temperature TE applications. Still, the phase transition in GeTe from a rhombohedral structure to a cubic structure near ∼700 K hinders its suitability of applications for energy harvesting. There are already some reported works on doping, multiple doping, entropy engineering, etc., to reduce the phase transition temperature of GeTe.373,384,385,387,388,399,407 These techniques can be applied to other materials that face similar phase transition problems in their operating temperature range.
(vii) It is desirable to understand the structure of the probable promising material for thermoelectric applications at the atomic scale using some advanced spectroscopic techniques combined with insights rendered by the results of first-principles calculations and machine learning interatomic potential approach. This will focus on identifying the accurate and appropriate contributions of the lattice part of thermal conductivity from the moment tensor potential (MTP) of the machine learning language approach. As a result, it is expected that the figure of merit (ZT) of such intriguing 2D materials may be compared or projected to the values obtained from experiments after suitable synthesis routes.454–456

Table Content:
Medha Rakshit	Medha Rakshit received her B. Sc. honours degree in Physics in 2017 from Bankura Christian College, affiliated with The University of Burdwan. She obtained her M. Sc. Degree in Physics from Jawaharlal Nehru University, New Delhi, in 2019. Currently, she is working as a research scholar at the Department of Physics, Indian Institute of Engineering Science and Technology, Shibpur, under the supervision of Prof. Dipali Banerjee.
Debnarayan Jana	Dr Debnarayan Jana (b. 1968) M. Sc. (Calcutta) Ph. D. (Raman Research Institute, Bengaluru/degree from JNU, New Delhi) is a Professor of Physics in University of Calcutta since 2012. His current research is focused on physical properties of 2D materials from Density functional theory (DFT). He was elected as a Fellow of West Bengal Academy of Science and Technology (WAST) in 2015. He has more than 170 papers in national and international peer reviewed journals. Of late, Govt. of India has honoured him with prestigious INSA Teachers Award (2020) for his accomplishments as a distinguished teacher over the years.
Dipali Banerjee	Prof. Dipali Banerjee, BSc (1978) in Physics from Hindu College (Delhi University), MSc (1980) from Delhi University, Ph. D. from Indian Association for the Cultivation of Science, (Jadavpur University) Kolkata (1990), Post Doc CSIR fellow, IACS, Kolkata (1990–1995), is a Professor in Department of Physics, Indian Institute of Engineering Science and Technology, Shibpur, since 2003. Her current research is focused on synthesis and characterization of nanomaterials (inorganic and organic) including Graphene and Carbon nanotubes and composites for green energy generation and environmental remediation.
